==PAGE:3==
Preface
This Master’s thesis on "Optimization and classification of EEG data for a simple BCI by using
different types of EEG acquisitions" has been conducted as part of the requirements for obtain-
ing a Master’s degree within Biomedical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark
(DTU).
The research presented in this thesis has taken place from September 2022 until March 2023,
and corresponds to a workload of 35 ECTS points.
The two main supervisors of this project were Sadasivan Puthusserypady from DTU and Asbjørn
Wulff Helge from UNEEG Medical A/S.
ii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
Anyone can suffer a spinal cord injury or stroke and risk paralysis. People living with paralysis
are challenged by even simple activities at home. A brain-computer interface (BCI) is one way
to help the person, but it must be affordable, work in the home, and overcome the current
shortcomings of BCIs based on steady-state visual evoked potentials (SSVEP). In this regard,
this project proposed a setup that does not depend exclusively on the visual cortex and at the
same time uses higher visual frequencies to improve comfort and reduce the risk of triggering
epileptic seizures. A hybrid setup combining SSVEP and auditory steady-state response (ASSR)
was proposed.
To meet the perspective of an affordable BCI, the project was started using
OpenBCI with a simple screen and ordinary headphones, and later compared with g.tec.
Two classification algorithms, a support vector machine (SVM) and a convolutional neural net-
work (CNN), were compared. The best data input and group of electrode channels were evaluated
by a statistical approach, while the best hyperparameters were found through an automated
search space.
The three experiments, ASSR, SSVEP and SSVEP-ASSR, were evaluated for
accuracy, sensitivity, specificity, precision and F1-score on a 3-fold split of the test set.
It turned out that OpenBCI had too many problems such as data packet loss and excessive
noise that made it unsuitable for this type of experiment and led to random classifications.
Further investigation showed that it was possible to improve the signal quality, but it required
a more time-consuming setup than g.tec and also could not prevent packet loss from occurring.
Therefore, it was only possible to draw conclusions about the SSVEP, ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR
experiments including the classifiers from the recordings from g.tec.
The parallel-acting hybrid SSVEP-ASSR setup was not able to shorten the detection time.
Instead, it increased classification complexity and led to lower accuracies than what was found
for the same system with SSVEP. The CNN model returned the highest overall and average
accuracy in all the experiments (SSVEP, ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR), and was therefore the most
optimal in the studied experiments.
On the other hand, the results from the SSVEP setup confirm that SSVEP generalizes well
between data sets which could be seen from a high accuracy (max. accuracy of 86.67 ± 4.71%)
on an unseen subject, and even better in a user-dependent setup (up to 100 ± 0.00% accuracy).
An analysis of the segmentation length found an optimal length of 4.0 s, and highlights the
possibility of further shortening the detection time in a user-dependent setup. A relatively high
accuracy in the SSVEP experiment was also achieved with input from just one electrode around
the temporal lobe. This opens up the possibility of using an implant from e.g. UNEEG Medical
A/S to serve several different purposes, e.g. BCI control.
Finally, a graphical user interface (GUI) was created to design SSVEP stimulation, perform
data acquisition, and finally separate and classify the SSVEP recordings. The GUI simplified
the process of SSVEP response verification and can hopefully be used in the future to easily
test different stimulus shapes, frequencies and colors and hopefully find a combination that is
comfortable for the user and leads to a low detection time with high accuracy.
iii
==PAGE:5==
Resumé
Enhver kan blive ramt af en rygmarvsskade eller slagtilfælde og risikere lammelse. Mennesker,
der lever med lammelser, er udfordret af selv simple aktiviteter derhjemme. En hjerne-computer
interface (BCI) er én måde at hjælpe personen på, men den skal være økonomisk overkommelig,
virke i hjemmet og overvinde de nuværende mangler ved BCIer baseret på steady-state visuelle
fremkaldte potentialer (SSVEP). I denne henseende foreslog dette projekt en opsætning, der ikke
udelukkende afhænger af den visuelle cortex og samtidig bruger højere visuelle frekvenser til at
forbedre komforten og reducere risikoen for at udløse epileptiske anfald. En hybrid opsætning, der
kombinerer SSVEP og auditiv steady-state respons (ASSR), blev foreslået. For at imødekomme
perspektivet om en prisvenlig BCI, blev projektet indledt med at anvende OpenBCI med en
simpel skærm og almindelige høretelefoner, og senere sammenlignet med g.tec.
To klassifikationsalgoritmer, en støttevektormaskine (SVM) og et konvolutionelt neuralt netværk
(CNN), blev sammenlignet. Det bedste datainput og gruppe af elektrodekanaler blev evalueret
ved en statistisk tilgang, mens de bedste hyperparametre blev fundet gennem et automatiseret
søgerum. De tre eksperimenter, ASSR, SSVEP og SSVEP-ASSR, blev evalueret med henblik på
nøjagtighed, sensitivitet, specificitet, præcision og F1-score på en 3-dobbelt opdeling af testsæt-
tet.
Det viste sig, at OpenBCI havde for mange problemer såsom tab af datapakker og overdreven
støj, der gjorde det uegnet til denne type eksperimenter og førte til tilfældige klassifikationer.
Yderligere undersøgelser viste, at det var muligt at forbedre signalkvaliteten, men det krævede en
mere tidskrævende opsætning end g.tec og kunne heller ikke forhindre pakketab i at opstå. Derfor
var det kun muligt at drage konklusioner om SSVEP-, ASSR- og SSVEP-ASSR-eksperimenterne
inklusive klassifikatorerne ud fra optagelserne fra g.tec.
Den parallelt virkende hybride SSVEP-ASSR opsætning var ikke i stand til at forkorte detek-
tionstiden. I stedet øgede den klassificeringskompleksiteten og førte til lavere nøjagtigheder end
hvad der blev fundet for det samme system med SSVEP. CNN-modellen returnerede den højeste
samlede og gennemsnitlige nøjagtighed i alle eksperimenterne (SSVEP, ASSR og SSVEP-ASSR),
og var derfor den mest optimale i de undersøgte eksperimenter.
Til gengæld bekræfter resultaterne fra SSVEP-opsætningen, at SSVEP generaliserer godt mellem
datasæt, hvilket kunne ses ud fra en høj nøjagtighed (maks. nøjagtighed på 86.67 ± 4.71%)
på et uset emne, og endda endnu bedre i en brugerafhængig opsætning (op til 100 ± 0.00%
nøjagtighed). En analyse af segmenteringslængden fandt en optimal længde på 4.0 s, og belyser
muligheden for at forkorte detektionstiden yderligere i en brugerafhængig opsætning. En relativ
høj nøjagtighed i SSVEP-eksperimentet blev desuden opnået ved input fra blot én elektrode
omkring tindingelappen. Det åbner muligheden for at anvende et implantat fra f.eks. UNEEG
Medical A/S til at tjene forskellige formål, f.eks. BCI styring.
Endelig blev der skabt en grafisk brugergrænseflade (GUI) til at designe SSVEP-stimulering, fore-
tage dataindsamling og til sidst separere og klassificere SSVEP-optagelserne. GUI’en forenklede
processen med verifikation af SSVEP-respons og kan forhåbentlig bruges i fremtiden til nemt at
teste forskellige stimuliformer, frekvenser og farver og forhåbentlig finde en kombination, der er
behagelig for brugeren og fører til en lav detektionstid med høj nøjagtighed.

Acknowledgment
First, I would like to thank my supervisors Asbjørn Wulff Helge and Sadasivan Puthusserypady
for always being very helpful and open to discussions. Our weekly meetings have been truly
appreciated and I am thankful for all the support and comments you have provided. I would also
like to thank the other members of our weekly meetings for good times in the office and useful
discussions. In particular, a big thank you to Cihan Uyanik for the many good suggestions and
constructive feedback given during this thesis.
Special thanks to UNEEG Medical A/S for providing the great help from Asbjørn and for giving
me the opportunity to use your facilities. Likewise I thank DTU for the equipment I borrowed
and the laboratories I used.
I am also grateful to all participants who wanted to participate in my experiments, their patience
and willingness. For some people even multiple times. Finally, I would like to thank my friends
and family for all their support and care they have shown at all times.
This thesis would never have been the same without the help, contribution and support from all
of you. Thank you!
Kgs. Lyngby, March 2023

Chapter 1
Introduction
The electroencephalography (EEG)-based brain-computer interface (BCI) has been used in med-
ical applications for a long time, but it has potential to expand far beyond that and give the
user much more control of its daily life [1]. Hippocrates (460-379 B.C.E.) was the first person
to truly put focus towards the brain as the center of intelligence, instead of the heart: "In these
ways I am of the opinion that the brain exercises the greatest power in the man" [2]. But it was
indeed the discovery by Hans Berger in 1924 that made it possible to use EEG to measure neural
activity from the brain [3]. Since then better measurement tools and knowledge about the brain
signals have become accessible, and today it is now possible to take the next step and set up an
EEG-based BCI. In an EEG-based BCI, brain signals are measured and interpreted in order to
control an external device, e.g. a computer. The first BCI applications were developed to help
people with disabilities to be able to communicate or using aiding equipment [1]. A study in
2013 by the Christopher & Dana Reeve Foundation revealed that 1 in 50 Americans live with
paralysis (approx. 5.2 million people) mostly caused by strokes followed by spinal cord injuries
[4]. An overview of the causes found from the study is seen in Figure 1.1.
(a)
(b)
Figure 1.1: A statistical overview of the number of people in the United States with paralysis.
(a) shows the distribution of causes of paralysis, while (b) shows the distribution of causes of
spinal cord injuries. The data is from [4].
People who suffer from movement disabilities due to cerebral palsy, spinal cord injury or other
disabilities that affect these people’s motor skills are greatly affected in their daily life [5]. In
order to improve their life quality, free them for constant assistance and give them the ability to
perform the simplest activities of the day, new systems and tools must be made. An EEG-based
BCI for smart home control is a step towards this direction with the possibility of controlling
anything from a wheelchair to a smart TV. Nevertheless, if EEG-based BCIs should be made for
smart home control, some major challenges must be confronted and solved.
1
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 1. Introduction
1.1. SSEP for smart home control
1.1
SSEP for smart home control
A future application that applies to both medical and non-medical is the smart home control.
For an EEG-based BCI to be used in a private home, the BCI must be user-friendly, have less
training time and high signal quality. A smart home control system based on SSVEP (steady-
state visual evoked potential), a subgroup of SSEP (steady state evoked potential), has been
suggested by many, e.g. Gao et al. [6]. SSVEPs are evoked oscillatory cortical responses that
uses the phenomenon of synchronization to the frequency of the brain [7], and can be recorded
from the scalp, non-invasive, over the visual cortex, with maximum amplitude at the occipital
region [8]. For this reason, the SSVEP is generally subject independent with a high signal-to-
noise ratio.
Subject-independent BCI systems are of high importance as it requires less training time and are
less prone to classification errors. Less training time is convenient for everyone, but in particular
for people with mental disorders [1].
SSVEPs have shown reliable performance in terms of
accuracy and response time, even with a small number of EEG channels and at a relatively high
information transfer rate (ITR) [9],[10]. Overall, the SSVEP-based BCI seems promising towards
the application of smart home control.
Challenges in SSVEP-based BCI systems for smart home control
SSVEP-based and many hybrid BCIs rely solely on the visual cortex, which causes some chal-
lenges in terms of long detection times and eye-fatigue.
The long detection time and stimulation duration are necessary to achieve a high accuracy as
long as the target classification is based on input of a single BCI system. In a SSVEP-based BCI
by Ge et al. [11], a stimulation duration of 3.9 s was needed to obtain the highest accuracy of
96.0% while a stimulation duration of 1.3 s led to an accuracy of 88.6%. Long detection times
are not ideal for long-term use and in particular not by people with motor disabilities who are
completely dependent on the BCI during the entire day. Furthermore, a long detection time can
contribute to the cause of fatigue and especially eye-fatigue.
Since a reliable SSVEP response requires the user’s attention at all times, eye-fatigue remains
a major challenge in SSVEP and real-life use.
Studies have shown that eye-fatigue can be
reduced by using high-frequency flickering stimuli (>25 Hz), and high frequency stimuli generally
do not cause stimulus-related seizures, in contrast to low frequency stimuli that could evoke
photosensitivity-based epileptic seizures [12]. Furthermore, Won and colleagues [12] discovered
that high-frequency stimuli also led to a significantly better visual comfort, but a simple monitor
with a 60 Hz refresh rate can only display a very limited number, if any, of high-frequency flickers.
Low-frequency stimuli (up to 12 Hz) have been used for most SSVEP BCI studies because they
have high amplitude and high SNR [13]. For real-world applications, a BCI must be comfortable
to use while still being stable and robust over time.
The combination of two SSEP techniques
The use of two parallel and non-invasive SSEP techniques may make it possible to create a BCI
that both uses high-frequency visual stimuli and also provides high accuracy. In addition to
SSVEP, this project will consider ASSR (auditory steady-state response). The ASSR responds
to the amplitude modulation (AM) frequency of the auditory stimulus by oscillating at the same
or resonant frequency [14]. However, an ASSR-based BCI has not yet proven to be a reliable
Page 2 of 77
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 1. Introduction
1.2. Related work
system that can be used in real-world applications. Generally because it may be too difficult
for the subjects to distinguish the auditory target stimulus from the non-target stimulus [14].
However, this project will look into whether a parallel combination of SSVEP and ASSR response
can increase the accuracy. A SSVEP-ASSR-based BCI has the advantage of not solely relying
on the visual cortex, plus the combination may allow for shortening the detection time despite
the use of visual stimuli frequencies above 12 Hz.
1.2
Related work
In literature, a number of hybrid EEG-based BCI systems have been proposed to improve the
performance of a BCI system for smart home control. This project focuses on a SSVEP-ASSR-
based system. The relevant articles are selected through a systematic literature search process
(see Figure 1.2) with some inclusion/exclusion criteria specified in Google scholar. Inclusion
criteria includes 6 databases from Elsevier, IEEE, IEEJ, Springer, PubMed and ScienceDirect.
A keyword criteria was set to include SSVEP in the paper title, as it is the main part of the setup
together with at least one of following words: ASSR, SSAEP, auditory and smart home. It must
be noted that an initial search with the keywords SSVEP and SSAEP in the paper title led to
just one match: Punsawad et al. [15]. A similar search with SSVEP and ASSR led to no results.
Finally, a constraint on the date of publishing was set to be between year 2012-2022 together
with a requirement of minimum 10 citations.
Figure 1.2: Systematic literature review search process.
The literature search process found a total of 9 papers, but only 7 were selected based on rele-
vance. All of the papers are listed in Table 1.1, but note that it was not possible to find any papers
that have created a SSVEP-ASSR-based setup. Suitable BCIs for smart home environments are
of interest which means short training time, high accuracy and multi-command options. Multiple
different hybrid BCIs have been suggested in literature to achieve this. The literature review in
Table 1.2 found two hereof, a combination of SSVEP and EMG, and a combination of SSVEP
and eye-gaze tracking.
Chai et al. [16] investigated a combination of SSVEP and EMG. SSVEP features were recognized
by using CCA and did the pre-detection, while EMG patterns from the single channel of tem-
poralis muscle were used to confirm the selected function. A total of 10 subjects participated, 5
healthy and 5 with paralysis. The average target selection in the SSVEP experiment was 97.5%
and 83.6% for the healthy and paralyzed subjects, respectively. The combination of SSVEP
and EMG improved the accuracy to 97.6% and 96.9% for the healthy and paralyzed subjects,
respectively.

Table 1.1: Relevant papers found from the systematic literature review (see Figure 1.2). Subj. is
short for Subjects. Elec. represents number of electrodes. Tar. stands for number of targets.
H represents Healthy. P stands for Paralysis. "*" notes that this experiment required SSAEP
pre-training.
Putze et al. [18] considered combining eye-tracking and SSVEP in an Augmented Reality (AR)
in 2019.
This uses the finding of [22], where it was found that SSVEP-based BCI was the
easiest to use and eye-tracking was the least tiring. Putze et al. showed that the hybrid BCI
system improved the classification accuracy from 76.1% and 82.1% on average for SSVEP and
eye-tracking, respectively, while the combination led to an average of 89.3%. The paper also
mentions some challenges in eye-tracking. One challenge is the calibration as some users wear
glasses which affects the calibrated eye-position. A second but general challenge for SSVEP is
the visual flickers that can become distracting for the user if he/she is not interested in activating
a command.
Instead of eye-gaze, Goel et al. [21] considered eye-blinks to make the user verify his/her selection
in favor of a more robust detection algorithm. The experiment worked in a series where 2 seconds
of SSVEP response was recorded. Auto-correlation was applied to the raw EEG data, followed
by a Fast Fourier transform (FFT) and a sum of power amplitudes around the target frequencies.
An adaptive threshold was then used to detect the SSVEP. If detected, a 4 second window was
given and if the user blinked thrice, the device selection would be confirmed. The accuracy was
found to be 100% but led to a long detection time of 6 s in total.
All of the BCIs mentioned above rely solely on the visual cortex. Therefore, in case of eye-
fatigue, eye-blinks, contact lenses, glasses etc., all sub-components will be affected. To avoid
this, Punsawad et al. [15] has proposed a steady-state auditory evoked potential (SSAEP)-based
BCI system to replace the SSVEP-system during periods of visual fatigue. Hence, it doesn’t
consider a parallel system as this project will try to implement. Punsawad et al. achieved an
average accuracy of 81 ± 5.18% (using 7 subjects) by placing a speaker in front of the subject. It
Page 4 of 77
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 1. Introduction
1.2. Related work
was noted that only highly experienced users could maintain the high level of attention needed
to obtain high accuracy. Without training, the accuracy was found to be 59 ± 9.34%.
Feature extraction & Classification
A major challenge in any classification task is the extraction of features and in particular the
selection and optimization of a classification algorithm.
A 2022 paper by Värbu et al.
[1]
investigated the most common feature extractions and classification algorithms in EEG-based
BCI systems. The results showed that the most common feature extractions are PSD (18%) and
Fourier transform (16%). In general, power spectral density (PSD) spectra are good features for
evoked potentials as they result in peaks specifically at the target frequencies in the frequency
domain.
Linear discriminant analysis (LDA) was found to be the most used classification algorithm (31%
of the studies), while support vector machine (SVM) was the second most (21% of the stud-
ies).
Putze and colleagues [18] used Canonical Correlation Analysis (CCA) to process the EEG signals
after having applied a bandpass filter from 1-35 Hz. CCA requires two sets of variables, here the
EEG signals and a group of sine and cosine functions representing the target frequency and the
first two harmonics. As a result, the SSVEP data is classified as the class that has the highest
correlation coefficient. However, Putze et al. set a requirement that the CCA coefficient must
be above 0.4, otherwise the eye-gaze result is returned which is classified by a nearest neighbor
approach. Similarly, Punsawad et al. [15], who created a SSAEP-based BCI system, uses the
amplitude of the PSD as features. It simply classifies the class based solely on the amplitude
values, instead of using a machine learning classification algorithm like Anindya et al. [19] that
used a SVM classifier.
A consequence of using just the amplitudes is that the algorithm is
expected to be less robust to noise.
This project will extract features of ASSR and SSVEP in the form of PSD and CCA, while the
classification is performed on the one hand by SVM, and on the other by a neural network to
compare the results. Signal processing and optimization will also be part of this process.
Page 5 of 77
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 1. Introduction
1.3. Problem statement
1.3
Problem statement
The number of people with disabilities increases dramatically every year [23], and we still have
not solved the major challenges in EEG-based BCIs for smart home control. Currently, most
BCIs for smart home control rely solely on the visual cortex, have long detection times, and
are likely to cause fatigue or worse; epileptic seizures. In the literature, SSVEP-based BCIs and
low-frequency stimuli are used in favor of strong signals and high accuracy, but a combination
of new BCI systems and better classification algorithms may provide the basis for a disabled
person to once and for all be in control of the simplest activities at home.
1.4
Research questions, objectives and goals
Based on the introduction and problem statement, the following research questions, objectives
and goals were defined:
Question 1:
How can a hybrid SSVEP-ASSR-based BCI be designed to improve a BCI
system for smart home control?
Objective:
The current hybrid SSVEP-ASSR-based BCIs work in series and are designed
to solve the problem of eye-fatigue, but not the long detection time. A long
detection time is not suitable for smart home control, so it must be investigated
whether a parallel-acting SSVEP-ASSR-based BCI can shorten the detection
time and improve the system for smart home control.
Question 2:
Which machine learning model is optimal for identification of target stimuli in
a smart home control setup using a hybrid SSVEP-ASSR-based BCI?
Objective:
As a smart home control setup is planned to work in the private home, it
is expected to be a noisier environment with cheaper equipment than those
carried out in laboratories.
The typical classification algorithm for evoked
potentials, SVM, may therefore not be ideal, hence it must be investigated
whether an SVM and/or a neural network can accurately identify the target
stimuli in a smart home control setup.
Question 3:
What are the potential advantages of using a SSVEP-ASSR-based BCI?
Objective:
At present time, it is difficult to know the performance level of combining
the auditory and visual stimuli in a BCI, as very limited literature exists.
SSVEP is widely used and shows reliable performance in terms of accuracy,
response time and a high ITR. On the other hand, ASSR scores lower accuracy
but solves some of the day-to-day problems associated with SSVEP. For this
reason, it is scientifically interesting to study if a hybrid SSVEP-ASSR-based
BCI can overcome some of the major obstacles of evoked potential-based BCIs,
such as eye-fatigue, long detection times and reliance on just the visual cortex.
Project goals
• To set up a simple EEG-based BCI that do not rely on just one type of SSEP stimuli but
use multiple to obtain a shorter detection time and high accuracy when using a BCI.
• To code and optimize a classification algorithm in Python that can be used in a BCI for
smart home control.

Chapter 1. Introduction
1.5. Outline of this thesis
1.5
Outline of this thesis
In this thesis, a total of 9 chapters have been written in order to address the problem statement
given in 1.3 and the three research questions in section 1.4. The first chapter was presented prior
to this outline as it included the introduction and related work. The content of the upcoming
chapters are described below.
Chapter 2 - Theory: presents the physiological background and a theoretical background on
the techniques, processing methods and classification algorithms.
Chapter 3 - GUI for EEG data collection: describes a graphical user interface (GUI)
created for this project to easily design SSVEP stimuli and collect data. It was used to verify
the response to the created visual stimuli.
Chapter 4 - Design of the BCI: presents the overall BCI design and how the visual and
auditory stimuli were designed. It also includes a verification on the SSVEP response.
Chapter 5 - Experiment: provides the experiment protocol and gives an insight on the
participants and the materials used for OpenBCI and g.tec, respectively.
Chapter 6 - Methods used: provides information on the scripts created for data collection.
Also the theory presented in chapter 2 is here used to find and define the final algorithms.
Chapter 7 - Results: presents the outcomes of the methodology together with other additional
investigations.
Chapter 8 - Discussion: discusses the findings from chapter 7 and the thesis in general. At
last it will present suggestions for future research.
Chapter 9 - Conclusions: sums up the most important findings of this thesis and makes the
final conclusion.

Chapter 2
Theory
2.1
Human-brain physiology
The brain is a complex organ and controls anything from thoughts to breathing and in general
any process that regulates our body. Today it is possible to measure signals from the brain,
signals that can be used to create BCI applications even for smart home control. To understand
how and which areas are of interest, we must first understand the basic anatomy and physiology
of the brain.
2.1.1
Brain anatomy
At a high level, the brain is divided into the cerebrum, cerebellum and the brain stem, while
on an evolutionary level it is divided into the reptilian brain, limbic brain and the neocortex.
The neocortex is unique in mammals, and is evolutionary speaking the newest addition to the
mammalian brain. The neocortex consists of two sides, hemispheres, that controls the opposite
side of the body.
Each hemisphere can be divided into four major lobes: frontal, parietal,
temportal and occipital (see Figure 2.1a).
(a) Brain lobes
(b) Brain cortex
Figure 2.1: Two figures (a) and (b). (a) shows the four lobes of the brain and other characteristics
of the brain. It is a modified version of [24] including information from [25, p.544]. (b) is the
cortex seen from the transverse plane based on information from [26].
Especially, the temporal and the occipital lobes are of interest for this project. The occipital
lobe processes visual input and visual tasks such as color detection, depth perception, and mo-
tion detection while the temporal lobe is mainly responsible for hearing, speech and learning
languages. The temporal lobe also processes visual information in the medial and anterior part
of the temporal lobe, but only visual recognition such as recognizing faces.
8
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.1. Human-brain physiology
2.1.2
Converting photons or phonons to electrical signals
The central nervous system (CNS) including the brain and the spinal cord is central to the
measurement of neural activity. Before it can process any information, it must receive input
from the peripheral nervous system (PNS) as illustrated in Figure 2.2a. Not only does the PNS
send information from the body to the brain, but it also carries out commands from the brain to
various parts of the body. This process is illustrated in Figure 2.2a. The PNS includes several
sensory systems with specific receptors to capture information from e.g. auditory and visual
stimuli. Auditory stimuli in form of sound waves going to the ear will set the basilar membrane
into motion and thus increase its mechanical energy. From there, the inner and outer hair cells
of the cochlea (receptors), transduce the energy into a signal that all neurons understand (the
definition of a receptor). To be specific, the motility of outer hair cells provides an additional
amplification of the basilar membrane, while the inner hair cells respond to these vibrations and
excite the large population of neurons upon which they synapse [25, p.537].
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.2: Two figures (a) and (b). (a) shows how information from the periphery is processed
and interpreted in the CNS. Source: [27]. (b) shows the receptor morphology and relationship
to ganglion cells in the visual and auditory systems. Inspiration from: [28].
In the visual system, there are two main types of photoreceptors that absorb the light that
reaches the cellular layers of the retina at the back of the eye: rods and cones [25, p.537]. The
signals from the rod contain no color information, but instead allow that person to see in dim
light. In daytime, three types of cones are active (red, green and blue). Based on their activity
ratio between the different cones, the brain is able to deduce the wavelength and hereby the
color of the input.
Figure 2.2b shows the steps from photoreceptors absorbing photons and
bipolar cells transmitting the signal from photoreceptors to ganglion cells. The retina has more
photoreceptors at the center of the eye (at the fovea) than in the periphery, and therefore the
center of the central visual field will be of higher resolution.
Page 9 of 77
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.2. Recording the brain signals
2.1.3
Neurons and the action potential
Figure 2.3: Illustration of the action
potential and the four most important
steps. Source: [29].
The receptors previously described are also known as
sensory neurons, but it is actually the pyramidal cells
(multipolar neurons) that are the usual targets for cor-
tical recording. These neurons have a cell body of 10-30
µm in diameter [30]. Neurons notably differ from other
cells by having an axon, dendrites, and a membrane that
use neurotransmitters or electrical events to open selec-
tive ion channels.
The dendrites receive inputs from
other neurons at junctional spots called synapses. This
process starts what can lead to an action potential. If
input stimuli overcome the -55 mV threshold, a neuron
will fire an action potential.
Action potentials travel
through the axon as information and are basically just
a temporary shift in the neuron’s membrane potential.
The whole process is usually described in four periods:
resting state, depolarization, repolarization and refrac-
tory period (hyperpolarization) as can be seen in Figure
2.3.
At rest, the membrane potential is -70 mV, but during depolarization, sodium gates open and the
electrochemical gradient causes the positively charged sodium to enter the cell, so the membrane
potential becomes positive (overshoot). Hence, the sodium channel inactivates while the potas-
sium channel opens and potassium ions leave the cell (repolarization). The potassium channels
close during the refractory period, but slowly, so another action potential cannot be generated.
Throughout the process sodium-potassium is active. After the refractory period, it reestablishes
the potential gradient by moving more sodium ions out than potassium ions in. The membrane
potential then returns to the resting potential.
The axon is covered by oligodendrocytes (glial cells) to form the myelin sheath that protects the
axon and speed up the conduction along the axon. When the action potential reaches the axon
terminal, it passes the signal to the next neuron. It is indeed this process that is important for
EEG measurements, since EEG measures postsynaptic potentials. Most synapses in the body
are chemical synapses. Here, the depolarization from the action potential causes the voltage-
dependent calcium gates to open. The presence of calcium initiates exocytosis (neurotransmitters
are secreted into the synaptic cleft), allowing neurotransmitters to diffuse from the presynaptic
neuron to the postsynaptic membrane, where they bind to receptors. The chemical signal can
cause the postsynaptic neuron to either depolarize (excitatory postsynaptic potential (EPSP)) or
hyperpolarize (inhibitory postsynaptic potential (IPSP)), thereby either increasing or decreasing
the likelihood of a postsynaptic action potential occurring. Nevertheless, both contribute to the
synaptic activity recorded as EEG (electroencephalography) [31].
2.2
Recording the brain signals
Several devices exist for neural recording and imaging techniques. They are usually compared in
terms of temporal and spatial resolution, invasive vs. non-invasive and price. Spatial resolution is
a measure of the smallest object the sensor can resolve, whereas the temporal resolution describes
the time to acquire the final data. The best device in terms of spatial and temporal resolution is
Page 10 of 77
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.2. Recording the brain signals
ECoG (electrocorticography) and µECoG devices, and are also less sensitive to artifacts such as
movements with a more robust signal-to-noise ratio than EEG [32]. However, the clear advantage
of EEG is that it is simple and safe, where ECoG requires a surgical placement of electrodes
on top of the cortex. A visualization of the different neural imaging techniques are shown and
compared in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: A visualization of different neural recording and imaging techniques based on their
temporal and spatial resolution. Source: [33].
Functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) is also of great interest as the technique is non-
invasive and result in images with good spatial resolution including structural and functional
details. Nevertheless, fMRI is not ideal for a BCI for smart home control due to its size, hardware
complexity, and high procurement and maintenance costs. EEG on the other hand is fast and
relatively easy to set up, and will be the method of choice for recording brain signals in this
project.
2.2.1
EEG
Electroencephalography (EEG) is a technique for non-invasive recording of the brain’s electrical
activity, which measures the field potential in the space around neurons in relation to a reference
electrode.
The graphic representation of the recordings is called electrogram and shows the
difference in voltage between two different cerebral locations, plotted over time [31]. The electrical
potential arise from synaptic activity from both EPSPs and IPSPs, from many neurons active
at the same time, and is recorded using small metal discs (called electrodes) attached to the
scalp. The more synchronized the firing of action potentials from a given population of neurons,
the easier it is to measure the EEG signal. [34, p.4]. Neural oscillations, or known as brain
waves, do this in a repeating cycle, resulting in electrical signals strong enough to be detected
by electrodes on the scalp. Some of the most common electrical patterns with specific signal
frequencies and amplitudes are alpha, beta, delta and gamma waves. Table 2.1 presents the
kinds of activities that greatly stimulate these neural oscillations. The brain of course includes
all types of frequencies, but some are more dominant than others depending on the cognitive
state. But in general, the higher the frequency of the wave, the more awake the person is, while
the amplitude is lower.
Page 11 of 77
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.2. Recording the brain signals

Cognitive state
Time signal (s)
Gamma
>35 Hz
Deeply focused
Beta
12-35 Hz
Busy, active thinking

8-12 Hz
Very relaxed, passive attention
Theta
4-8 Hz
Deeply relaxed, inward focused
Delta
0.5-4 Hz
Sleep
Table 2.1: The five most common neural oscillations including their specific frequency bands,
cognitive characteristics and typical EEG signals. The signals are from [35].
The recorded EEG signals are usually quite small and often measured in microvolts (µV). There-
fore, it has been standard to use a gel made of Ag/AgCl to ensure a good quality recording by
improving conduction while reducing the impedance of the skin electrode. An alternative is dry
electrodes, which are more convenient for home use, but is known to have a higher impedance,
increased signal instability, and more sensitivity to movement artifacts.
However, the brain’s response to a stimulus can be strengthened by repeating the stimulus
numerous times. The recording of such events are called ERPs (Evoked Response Potential),
and specifically for visual stimuli they are called VEPs (visual evoked potentials). Before starting
a VEP measurement using EEG, it is a good procedure to check the signal quality through alpha
waves. Both alpha waves and VEPs are usually strongest at the occipital lobe, which handles
vision.
EEG electrode placement
Figure 2.5: The international
10-20 system for electrodes
placement. Source: [36].
The electrodes on the scalp are typically distributed according to
the international 10-20 system to ensure reproducibility and to
efficiently compare EEG studies. The system ensures that the
entire scalp is covered by electrodes, from the nasion to the in-
ion and from the left preauricular point to the right preauricular
point, hence 10 and 20 refer to 10% and 20% of these distances,
respectively. Each electrode is marked with a letter and a number.
The letter indicates at which lobe the electrode is placed, either
Fp (pre-frontal), F (frontal), T (temporal), P (parietal) and O
(occipital). C (the central line in the brain) is also used as iden-
tification, but it is not an actual lobe. In addition, the number
indicates which hemisphere and the distance to the central line of
Page 12 of 77
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.3. Brain-computer interfaces
the brain. Therefore, odd and even numbers represent the left and right hemispheres, respec-
tively, while higher numbers are furthest away from the central line. Electrodes at the central line
are marked with "z" to represent zero. This project uses the EEG electrode cap from OpenBCI
and g.tec, both following the 10-20 system. The electrodes covering the primary auditory and
visual area are of interest. Figure 2.1a and 2.5 show that following electrodes are particularly of
interest for this project: O1, O2, Oz, T3 (T7) and T4 (T8). Please note that the Oz electrode
cannot be selected with the OpenBCI cap. The 21-channel electrode cap from OpenBCI uses
dry EEG comb electrodes, while g.tec uses g.Ladybird active electrodes. More details on the
individual systems are specified in Chapter 6.
2.3
Brain-computer interfaces
A brain-computer interface (BCI) is a computer-based system that allows the user to control
an external device by measuring CNS activity. The main applications of a BCI are to either
replace, restore, improve, supplement or enhance natural CNS output and thereby change the
ongoing interactions between the CNS and its external or internal environment [37, p. 3]. This
is in particular useful for people disabled by neuromuscular disorders such as amyotrophic lateral
sclerosis, cerebral palsy, stroke, or spinal cord injury. In principle, any type of brain signal could
be used to control a BCI system, but this project will make an example of a simple BCI for
smart home control that uses SSVEP and ASSR as input.
The design of a BCI includes 5 steps: signal acquisition, preprocessing, feature extraction, clas-
sification (feature translation) and device control (applications). The steps are shown in Figure
2.6.
Figure 2.6: The basic design and operation of a BCI system using auditory and visual stimuli.
Once the signals are acquired from the scalp, they will take part in the overall step named signal
processing. The signal processing step includes a three-step procedure [37, p. 129]:
• Signal preprocessing to reduce noise and to enhance relevant aspects of the signals
• Extract and process features to define a feature vector for the classification step
• Classification to define a model based on input features that can translate input features
into device control commands
Each step listed in Figure 2.6 will be described in the upcoming sections.
Page 13 of 77
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.4. Signal acquisition
2.4
Signal acquisition
The signals acquired in this project are generated in response to the presentation of visual
(SSVEP) and auditory (ASSR) stimuli. Both SSVEP and ASSR are described in sec. 2.4.1 and
2.4.2, respectively.
2.4.1
SSVEP
Steady-state visual evoked potentials (SSVEPs) are brain signals that occur in response to fo-
cusing on a visual stimulus that flickers at a fixed frequency. The most popular technique in
SSVEP is frequency tagging, where the target stimulus (and its frequency) can be identified as
the dominant frequency in the frequency spectrum of SSVEPs [8]. A large part of the visual
cortex is allocated to processing the central visual field, and therefore the target stimulus will
dominate the spectrum from channels at the visual cortex, since the target is located in the
center of the visual field, where visual acuity is at its highest [8].
The visual stimuli
The visual stimuli in SSVEP can be presented either as blinking light emitting diodes (LEDs)
or flickers on a computer monitor [8]. A computer monitor allows more customization and easy
to change interface, but it is limited by the refresh rate of the monitor. For instance, a monitor
with a refresh rate of 60 Hz can only display flickers that are integer divisble by 60, leading to
only three flickers (in the alpha band of 8-13 Hz). These are 8.57 Hz (7 frames per period), 10
Hz (6 frames per period) and 12 Hz (5 frames per period). Furthermore, the detection of the
target stimulus can be improved by using the harmonic components of the flicker frequencies
in the spectrum, as shown by Müller et al. [38], and therefore can those frequencies also not
be used in the design of the BCI [39]. Not only does this limit the appliances of the BCI, but
also limit the ITR (information transmission rate) which could otherwise be easily improved by
increasing the number of targets [40]. The ITR is calculated by the following formula:
ITR = 60
t

log2 N + Acc log2 Acc + (1 −Acc) log2 Acc1 −Acc
N −1

,
(2.1)
where t is the sampling time, Acc is the correct rate, and N is the number of classifications
[41].
Instead, multiple flicker frequencies can be generated by varying the number of frames in each cy-
cle, e.g. between 5 and 6 frames, corresponding to 12 and 10 Hz: ’1110001110011100011100111...’
where ’1’s and ’0’s represent white and black on the monitor as done by Wang et al. [40]. So the
signal stimulus signal s at frequency f can be calculated as in eq. 2.2:
s(f, i) = square [2πf(i/RefreshRate)] .
(2.2)
From this method, a flicker at any frequency up to half of the monitor refresh rate can be realized
[8], and this opens the possibility of using simple and cheap PC monitors.
An important thing to be aware of during SSVEP measurements is fatigue and habituation.
Fatigue is a natural consequence of looking at a screen for a long time, but the user can also
get used to the repeated stimulus (habituation), leading to a progressive decrease in response.
Page 14 of 77
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.5. Preprocessing
However, whether habituation occurs during SSVEP stimulation or how to distinguish it from
fatigue effects in SSVEP has rarely been investigated [42]. Multiple algorithms and different
thresholds for task selection may be needed for day and night to handle both fatigue and habit-
uation. Furthermore, appropriate breaks must be implemented between measurements during
data collection, also to avoid fatigue and habituation.
2.4.2
ASSR
Similar to SSVEP, auditory steady-state response (ASSR) are brain signals that occur in re-
sponse to periodically repeated auditory stimulus, typically a sinusoidally modulated tone [43].
Hence, the idea is to pulse the stimulus volume up and down, instead of using a steady volume,
and hereby stimulate certain auditory neurons. The stimulated nerves will now pulse at the
modulation frequency, seen by a peak of energy in the frequency domain, along with its higher
harmonics.
The auditory stimuli
The auditory signal can be presented in different types and from different spatial locations. One
type is the SAM signal s1(t), as used in a conventional ASSR BCI application by Higashi et al.
[44], and obtained as
s1(t) = sin(2πfct) sin(πfmt),
(2.3)
where t is the time-course, fc is a carrier tone frequency, and fm is an envelope modulation
frequency [45]. Higashi et al. [44] used the SAM signal to design a two commands interface
where the user was equipped with headphones, and the sound stimuli was presented one-by-
one from its own spatial direction. Hence, a command could easily be selected by the user by
concentrating on one of the directions, while ignoring the other. In this project, the auditory
system is used as a parallel verification technique and therefore two commands might be sufficient
to verify targets selected during SSVEP from either the left or right side of the screen.
Alternatively, the auditory stimuli can be periodic clicks with the same number of ticks as the
frequency of the flickering button [45]. A click stimulus is easier to attend or ignore, and especially
if it follows the frequency of the flicker. However, it can be difficult to distinguish the auditory
stimuli from each other if high-frequency flicker is used, plus the number of speakers must match
the number of commands.
2.5
Preprocessing
In offline analysis of EEG signals, multiple preprocessing steps typically includes: downsample the
data, filtering, re-reference data, data segmentation, inspection of electrodes and segmentations,
reject noisy channels and segments, run ICA (independent component analysis) to remove noisy
components and save the preprocessed data.
If the goal was to get the best offline analysis, the previous steps would be ideal. However, this
project aims to prepare for a BCI for real-time applications, and therefore the offline analysis is
set up to match an online analysis as much as possible and always keep the computational load
in mind. For this reason, noisy channels will not be removed unless all the signals are bad or
something went wrong. The data will not be downsampled as it is already below 512 Hz and
instead of ICA, CCA (canonical correlation analysis) will be used. Prior knowledge of the general
Page 15 of 77
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.5. Preprocessing
signal characteristics of the SSVEP and ASSR methods (see sec. 1.1 and 2.4) is in favor of CCA
as it can be set up to use the correlations between the expected harmonics and the actual data.
Therefore, the signal preprocessing steps for this project can be divided into:
• Filtering
• Artifact removal
• Segmentation
2.5.1
Filtering
The obtained EEG signals are typically affected by both noise, n(t), and the power line interfer-
ence signal, p(t). Hence, the obtained signal, x(t), is given by:
x(t) = d(t) + p(t) + n(t),
(2.4)
where d(t) is the desired EEG signal. Filters can either be applied before or after the signal is
digitized to eliminate frequencies outside the frequency range of interest and get closer to the
actual d(t) signal [37, p. 129]. A filter could be classified as either high-pass, low-pass, band-
stop, or band-pass. The ideal cases of such filters are illustrated in the appendix in Figure B.1
[46].
A low-pass filter removes high frequencies and lets only low frequencies pass, while high-pass
filters do the exact opposite. Band-pass filters allow only a band-limited portion of the frequency
content to be passed through the filter, while a band-stop filter removes a specified frequency
range [46]. An ideal low-pass filter is defined as following:
H(f) =
(
1,
if |f < fcut|.
0,
otherwise,
(2.5)
where H(f) is the filter, f is the frequency and fcut is the cut-off frequency.
The impulse
response of an ideal filter is non-causal and thus it cannot be used practically for online applica-
tions. Instead, the filter types in Figure B.1 can be implemented using digital filters, either FIR
(finite impulse response) or IIR (infinite impulse response) filters, but will include a passband,
transitionband and a stopband as shown in Figure 2.7.
Digital filters
In digital filters, there are two categories of discrete-time filters: FIR and IIR. FIR and IIR filters
differ in particular in their impulse response. The FIR filter has a finite impulse response and
becomes 0 at some point, whereas an IIR filter will decay over time but never reach a steady-state
value. The output of using these two filters is expressed below:
FIR:
y(n) =
N
X
k=0
bkx(n −k),
IIR:
y(n) =
N
X
k=0
bkx(n −k) −
N
X
k=0
aky(n −k),
where bk and ak are the amplitudes, n is the current point in input, N is the filter order, k is
the k point in the filter, x is the input signal, and y is the output signal.
Page 16 of 77
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.5. Preprocessing
In an IIR filter, the transfer function depends on the weighted sum of past inputs and outputs
(recursion), while a FIR filter depends only on the weighted sum of past inputs.
There are
some advantages and disadvantages for each of the two filter types. FIR filters are always stable
and have a constant phase and consistent delay at different frequencies, whereas IIR filters
can sometimes be unstable and have unequal delays [46]. Nevertheless, an IIR filter has the
advantage that for a similar filter roll off as a FIR, a lower filter order or number of terms can
be used [46]. Because of this, fewer calculations are required to achieve the same result, making
an IIR filter much faster, which is usually better for embedded/real-time systems. In order to
achieve a specific behavior, suitable filter coefficients must be found. This process is called filter
design. The selected filter coefficients affect the passband, transitionband, stopband and amount
of ripples (see Figure 2.7).
Figure 2.7: Graphical representation of magnitude characteristics of a low-pass filter.
As eq. 2.5 suggests, the frequencies within the passband are ideally not changed (gain amplitude
is 1) while the frequencies in the stopband are set to 0. To achieve a similar result using either
a FIR or IIR filter, a narrow transition band must be used but the narrower it is, the higher
the implementation complexity and the amount of ripples. For this reason, the ideal frequency
response is unattainable as it produces excessive passband ripple in both the passband and
stopband. Besides, an IIR bandpass filter, this project will use notch filters. Notch filters are
very narrow bandstop filters and can e.g. be used to remove the 50 Hz power line interference
coming from an AC (alternating current) output source.
The filters must be applied before
data segmentation. The filter design process can be highly reduced using Python and limited to
typically specifying the filter type, frequency range of interest (for bandpass), the filter order, and
the sampling frequency. Note that the sampling frequency,fs, limits the maximum frequency,
fmax, as described by the Nyquist theorem: fmax ≤fs/2.
In Python, several IIR notch filters have been generated and compared by varying the quality
factor, Q. They are shown in Figure 2.8
Page 17 of 77
==PAGE:32==

(b)

The dashed lines indicate the minimal criterion for acceptance, in this case that no values outside
40-60 Hz may be attenuated more than 5%. (b) is the impulse response of the IIR 50 Hz notch
filter with Q=10.
The IIR notch filter with Q = 10 is seen to follow the minimal criterion for acceptance (a maximal
reduction of 5.0% on frequencies outside the 40-60 Hz range).
Since bandpass filters will also be applied in this project, a Butterworth and a Chebyshev type
II bandpass filters are compared in Figure 2.9.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.9: A comparison between two bandpass filters (Butterworth order=9 and Chebyshev-II
order=13). The amplitude gain is seen on (a), while (b) shows the impulse response of each of
the two bandpass filters.
Figure 2.9 clearly shows some of the advantages and disadvantages of the individual filters.
For example, the Chebyshev-II filter has no ripples in the passband and a narrower transition
band than a Butterworth filter. Instead, it has far more exaggerated stopband ripples than the
Butterworth filter. A Chebyshev type I filter rolls off faster than type II, but has ripples in the
passband.
2.5.2
Artifact removal
EEG does not just capture bioelectrical activity from the visual and auditory system, but also
from all the other neurons, such as muscle activity (electromyogram), eye activity (electro-
oculogram) and heart activity (electrocardiogram). These artifacts are not represented by unique
frequencies and must be removed or reduced by other means than normal filtering. For this
Page 18 of 77
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.5. Preprocessing
reason, this project will investigate two ways in which the influence of artifacts can be reduced;
common-average referencing (CAR) and canonical correlation analysis (CCA).
Common-average referencing
The CAR spatial filter is data-independent and works by computing the global mean of all the
channels at each time point and then subtracting that mean from each individual channel at
that time point. This process can reduce the impact of artifacts that appear across all channels,
e.g. 50 Hz power line interference [37, p.130].
Canonical correlation analysis
Canonical correlation analysis (CCA) is a statistical method for identifying the correlations
between two multivariate data sets. The method combines the functions of spatial filtering and
feature selection and is generally efficient, simple and easy to use. The low computational cost
in CCA gives it a huge advantage over ICA for real-time BCI applications, as BCIs require
artifact removal methods that are automatic and of low computational cost [47].
CCA can
be implemented in this project by denoting the acquired set of EEG signals as x(t), while the
second set of signals y(t) includes a set of periodic signals that matches the number of stimulation
frequencies including subharmonics. Both sine and cosine functions are included. For a specific
stimulation frequency f, the set of periodic functions will look like this [41]:
y(t) =

...

where fs is the sampling frequency. N is the number of sampling points, and Nh is the number

This project uses 12, 20 and 30 Hz as visual stimulation frequencies, and 35, 40 and 45 Hz as
auditory stimulation frequencies. Hence, the PSDs of the y(t) set of signals will look like Figure
2.10.
Figure 2.10: The PSDs of the y(t) set of signals where following stimulation frequencies are

Since this project plans to transform the signals into PSDs, it will only be necessary to include

Page 19 of 77
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.6. Feature extraction
2.5.3
Segmentation
Segmentation of data was performed to separate data into individual trials and into following
data frame structure: [no. of targets, no. of channels, no. of sampling points, no. of iterations]
for the SSVEP experiment. The data structure for both the ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR experiment
is: [no. of targets, no. actuals, no. of channels, no. of sampling points, no. of iterations] (see
code in sec. F.2.1). The epoch length was set to match the stimulation duration, but if the
number of sampling points did not match the expected number, the data were processed as
described in sec. 6.2.1. In the analysis (sec. 7.3) different epoch lengths were examined in the
search of a shorter detection time.
2.6
Feature extraction
SSVEP-based BCIs and other VEP-based BCIs have some well-known characteristics, and there-
fore a logical starting point would be to use the proposed features by the related work in sec.
1.2. The related work found in particular PSD and CCA to be common features in VEP-based
BCIs. A combination of SSVEP and ASSR as a hybrid BCI is hardly found in the literature,
and in such exploratory situations where little is known about the optimal feature choice, it is
preferable to evaluate multiple features and afterwards eliminate the features based on relevance
[37, p.133]. For this reason, both PSD and CCA will be compared as features and classified by
a SVM classifier (see sec. 6.4.2).
2.7
Classification
This project will consider and use two supervised classification algorithms that are both capable
of separating non-linear data: SVM and a neural network.
A neural network is a subset of
machine learning, and hereby follow its definition: an algorithm that is able to learn from data
[48]. All machine learning algorithms have settings called hyperparameters. Usually, the best
hyperparameters are found in a grid search (an iteration over different values of hyperparame-
ters). The central challenge in machine learning is to create an algorithm that performs well on
new, unobserved inputs. This ability is known as generalization. The hyperparameters in both
the SVM and neural network will be tuned during optimization in order to obtain the lowest
possible generalization error (test error). Two of the central challenges in machine learning are
underfitting and overfitting. Underfitting occurs when the model is unable to achieve a suffi-
ciently low training error. Overfitting occurs when the gap between the training and test error
is too large. This will be evaluated in the analysis section. But first, more details about SVM
and neural networks are described below.
2.7.1
Support vector machine
Support vector machines (SVM) is a kernel-based supervised classification/regression algorithm
with a sparse solution, so that all predictions for new inputs depend only on the kernel function
evaluated at a subset of the training data points [49, p.326]. SVM is a decision machine, so it
does not provide posterior probabilistic outputs as relevance vector machines (RVM). Classically,
SVM is defined for a two-class classification problem using a linear model of the form
y(x) = wTϕ(x) + b,
(2.7)
Page 20 of 77
==PAGE:35==

tion, and b is the bias. Here, it is assumed that the
training data is linearly separable in feature space.
The great advantage with SVM is that it can easily
be applied to non-linear situations.
In both situations, SVM tries to maximize the mar-
gin, which is defined to be the smallest distance be-
tween the decision boundary and any of the samples

Many data sets include outliers and therefore SVM also includes a so-called slack variable, ξn ≥0

is the case where the slack variable is not used for classification. If the slack variable is included,
the distance between the observations and the threshold is called a soft margin.
The slack
variable ξn dictates the degree to which the constraint on the nth data point can be violated.

where C > 0 is the regularization weight that controls the trade-off between the slack variable
penalty and the margin.
The best soft margin is found through cross-validation and evaluated on best classification. When
the threshold is determined using a soft margin, a soft margin classifier (also called support
vector classifier) is used. So far, only linear separable cases have been described, but if the
data set is not linearly separable, support vector machines are used as classifier. SVM uses a
so-called kernel trick (see Figure 2.11 to visualize the concept behind the classification).
Page 21 of 77
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.7. Classification
The kernel trick
The kernel trick is a simple method to make SVM capable of classifying non-linear data sets.
The idea is to project (not transform) the non-linear data into a higher dimensional space where
it can be linearly separated by a hyperplane (the term hyperplane is primarily used when the
separation cannot be drawn).
This is mathematically achieved by using Lagrangian, so the
obtained predictive function y using a kernel function k(x, x′) becomes [49, p.329]
y(x) =
N
X
n=1
antnk(x, xn) + b,
(2.11)
where tn is the corresponding target value to the feature vector, xn, and an is simply a multi-
plier.
There exist many kernel functions, but the radial-basis function (RBF) and the polynomial kernel
are the most used ones. They are defined as following in Scikit-learn [50]:
RBF :
exp

−γ∥x −xn∥2
,
(2.12)
Polynomial :
(γ ⟨x, xn⟩+ r)p,
(2.13)
where γ > 0 configures the sensitivity to differences in feature vectors (x and xn), r adjusts
the independent term in the kernel function (by default r = 0). p is the degree of the polyno-
mial.
The kernel functions are used to systematically find support vector classifiers in higher dimen-
sions. If p = 1 in the polynomial kernel, the relationships between each pair of observations is
computed in 1-dimension, which will be used to find a support vector classifier (the threshold).
A good value of p is found through cross-validation. The RBF kernel, on the other hand, finds
support vector classifiers in infinite dimensions.
Hyperparameters
To use the kernel SVM in Scikit-learn in Python, following hyperparameters must be set by the
user:
• The regularization parameter C
• The type of kernel function
• Additional parameter(s) to the kernel function, e.g. degree of polynomial kernel and kernel
coefficient (γ)
2.7.2
Neural network
A neural network is a method in artificial intelligence, inspired by the human brain, in which a
network of interconnected neurons is created. Limited human assistance is required as a neural
network works as an adaptive system where weighted connections between neurons are auto-
matically modified. This step is called learning. Learning in a neural network can be classified
into supervised learning, unsupervised learning, and reinforcement learning. This project will
consider a feed-forward, supervised convolutional neural network (CNN). CNN is an area within
deep learning that specializes in pattern recognition.
Page 22 of 77
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.7. Classification
A standard artificial neural network consists of interconnected neurons that are distributed
in three layers: an input layer, one or several hidden layers and an output layer (see Figure
6.9).
The input layer receives input in various forms, while the hidden layers perform computations
on the weighted inputs and thereby find relationships between the features in the input layer.
Each hidden unit in the hidden layers increases the complexity of the model. Too many hidden
units and layers can therefore cause the model to create non-existent relationships. The result
of this is overfitting and should be looked out for in the neural network design. The output layer
then delivers the outcome of the calculations and extractions.
Each neuron in the CNN consists of five parts: 1) some inputs with 2) some weights, 3) a bias,
4) a sum, and 5) an activation function, just like the standard ANN, and expressed by
z = f
 N
X
i=1
xi × wi + b
!
,
(2.14)
where z is the output, f is the activation function, xi is the ith input, wi is the ith weight, and b
is the bias.
The difference in a CNN relies in the hidden layers that includes the three characteristic layers:
convolutional layer, pooling layer and dense layer. Each of the layers are here described:
1. The convolutional layer is sometimes called the feature extractor layer as filters/kernels
are applied to every possible position on the input matrix in order to discover features
(and pattern recognition). For this reason, the generated matrix is called a feature map.
The feature map depends on the kernel size and the stride (number of moving steps in
convolution).
The typical activation function within the convolutional layers is ReLU
(Rectified Linear Unit) that sets all negative values to 0 [51].
2. In the pooling layer, values in the input feature map is integrated either by average maxi-
mum pooling or maximum pooling. Hereby, the number of parameters is diminished which
decreases the overfitting of the model.
3. The final part is the dense layer, also known as the fully connected layer, which involves
weights, biases, and neurons.
This means that all the neurons in the dense layer are
connected and receive information from all the neurons in the previous layer. The final step
is to send the information through an activation function (logistic for binary classification
and softmax for multiclassification), which finally ends up in the output layer that gives a
probability of each class. Therefore, the number of neurons in the output layer must match
the number of classes.
The output vector will finally be compared to the expected output.
Based on the resulting
error, the weights are adjusted to better match the actual output to the desired output. When
the neural network is finally trained, a model is created that models non-linear and complex
relationships between input and output data.
The classification in this project is performed via the softmax function that takes the CNN
produced feature vector, fCNN(y|x), as input and returns the probability of producing the label
y as [52]:
Page 23 of 77
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.7. Classification
softmax(y|x) =
exp(fCNN(y|x))
P|Y|
y′∈Y exp(fCNN(y′|x))
,
(2.15)
where Y is the set of all labels in the data set. The probabilities on all labels sums to 1, and
therefore the predicted class is determined as the maximum value.
During training, the loss between the predicted and actual class is computed by the categorical
cross-entropy (CCE) loss function. It is given by
CCE(y, ˆy) = −1
N
N
X
n=1
(yn log(ˆyn) + (1 −y) log(1 −ˆyn)),
(2.16)
where ˆy is the output distribution, and N is the number of training samples.
In addition, a loss optimization algorithm is used to find the minimum loss. Similar to [52], the
Adam optimizer will be used. It is an extension of the stochastic gradient descent that uses an
adaptive learning rate. Too small learning rates can result in a slow gradient descent and the risk
of getting stuck in a local minimum, while too high learning rates can overshoot the minimum
and never converge. The Adam optimizer is expressed by:
Loss = CCE(y, ˆy) + λ∥fCNN(w))∥2
2,
(2.17)
where w are the weights of the network and λ is the scaling factor for the weight update (equiv-
alent to the learning rate in Keras), basically the step size to update the parameters.
Hyperparameters
To setup a CNN using Keras in Python, following hyperparameters must be set by the user
[53]:
• Number of layers (convolutional and dense)
• Number of neurons/filters in each layer
• Number of kernels and size in each convolutional layer
• Activation function after each layer
• Regularization method (e.g. dropout or decay scaling)
• Weight regularization/Learning rate
• Batch size
• Epochs
Page 24 of 77
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 2. Theory
2.7. Classification
2.7.3
Optimization
The process of tuning hyperparameters is in many cases considered an experimental process,
especially in neural networks. Grid search is often used to search for optimal hyperparameters
in machine learning classifiers, as it makes a complete search over a given subset of the hyper-
parameters space [54]. Nevertheless, it is very computational expensive and requires a small
dimensional space. To overcome the boundary issue, other methods such as random search and
genetic algorithm are of interest. A genetic algorithm is inspired by an evolutionary approach
where it simulates the process of natural selection, including mixing of genomes and mutations.
It runs through a specified number of generations and selects the n top individuals as the parents
of the next generation, all evaluated on a fitness score set to be e.g. accuracy (more about the
algorithm is described in sec. B.1).
An alternative to genetic algorithms is Optuna [55]. It works through a statistical approach
where it by default uses a Bayesian optimization algorithm (Tree-structured Parzen Estimator)
to estimate a promising area of hyperparameters [56]. The general algorithm is seen in alg. 1.
A history record of the trials is saved and used to define a smaller and smaller search space. At
the same time, the search is automatically done over multiple threads to reduce the computation
time.
Algorithm 1 TPESampler (Tree-Structured Parzen Estimator) [56]
Initialize random subset of hyperparameters
while number of trials not complete do
Evaluate hyperparameters and sort them by scores
Split hyperparameters into two groups, x1 and x2, based on a predefined quantile
Model l(x1) and g(x2) into estimated densities using Parzen estimators
Locate hyperparameters with highest expected improvement [lowest l(x1)/g(x2)]
Define new subset of hyperparameters based on highest expected improvement
Trial no. = Trial no. + 1
end while
In this project, the hyperparameter search space is quite large, and therefore Optuna or genetic
algorithms are likely to be a better option than grid search. The performance and computation
time using grid search, a genetic algorithm and Optuna are compared in sec. 6.5.1.
Page 25 of 77
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 3
GUI for EEG data collection
A Brain Computer Interface includes in fact three very important steps: first the necessary
stimuli must be designed and created. Secondly, EEG data using the stimuli must be collected
and used to define and create a classification model, and finally the BCI must be set up to
do online classifications. This Graphical User Interface (GUI) includes the first two steps and
was created during this thesis. The GUI itself was mainly used to collect data for smaller tests
and comparisons between SSVEP stimuli color, sizes, and number of stimuli, but can in fact be
used by anyone with interests in doing SSVEP experiments. The main menu is seen in Figure
3.1 and separated into following three modes: Initial setup, Data collection and Create
model.
Figure 3.1: Main menu of EEG data collection GUI.
The GUI is created using PyQT in Python and requires certain packages, specified in the code
to run (the code: GUI-SSVEP-experiment-creator).
3.1
Initial setup
The Initial setup is named initial as it is a necessary step, before the SSVEP experiment data
collection can begin. Inside the Initial setup window, there are two options: 1) Create a new
flicker, or 2) Design SSVEP experiment. By selecting either 1) or 2) the window in Figure 3.2b
and 3.2c will pop up, respectively.

Create flicker
This option allows the user to customize a new flicker by specifying the frequency, color, object

the flicker video with the shortest duration. The specified Stimulus duration must therefore be
shorter than the maximum possible. If not, a warning message will occur and inform the user.
An example of a few warning messages are seen in Figure C.1.

duration, Cue time, Rest trials and Rest sessions only allow floating values. Sessions

only allows integers. As a result, letters or invalid characters cannot be typed in the input field.

3.2
Data collection
The Data collection mode allows the user to collect EEG data from the already designed
SSVEP experiments (see sec. 3.1.2). Requirements for the data collection include: Python,
OpenVibe Acquisition Server, and OpenVibe Designer. The OpenVibe Acquisition Server handles
the acquisition of data from a selected device, while OpenVibe Designer handles the storage of
the data and exporting it to a selected file format. Hence data collection is divided into following
necessary steps:
1. Open and run OpenVibe Acquisition Server
2. Run the Python GUI and select SSVEP paradigm
3. Open OpenVibe Designer, define saving path (copy from Python GUI) and start recording
4. Start measurement in Python GUI
5. Stop OpenVibe Designer recording
6. Stop OpenVibe Acquisition Server
The Data collection window can be seen in Figure 3.5.

3.3. Create model
The SSVEP data collection allows to browse the designed SSVEP experiment. Python then
automatically reads the info file in the folder and sets up the experiment accordingly. Before
starting the measurement, the steps defined in sec. 3.2 (also seen in more details in Figure 3.5)
must be fulfilled. To make it easier, the GUI produces the exact saving path, which must be
copied and inserted as the filename in the CSV File Writer in OpenVibe.
If the acquisition server is not running, the measurement window closes automatically. After
a completed measurement, the GUI creates a folder named Recordings in the selected SSVEP
experiment folder, where it saves the recording as a csv file. The csv file includes the channel
columns, a timestamp column and an Event Id column that includes markers of when a trial
started and ended (see more details about the markers in sec. 6.1.2).
The downside, of course, is that multiple programs add complexity and that OpenVibe only works
on Windows and Linux [57]. A Python package such as Brainflow can handle data acquisition,
data storage and export all in Python, but it is only compatible with newer EEG acquisition
devices such as OpenBCI Cyton/Ganglion/Cyton Daisy or g.tec Unicorn [58]. An option to
choose between OpenVibe or Brainflow in the GUI could be of interest, but since OpenVibe
is compatible with both new and older devices (or through Lab Streaming Layer (LSL)), only
OpenVibe will be part of the first version.

Create model
The Create model mode allows the user to first process and segment the data, and afterwards

3.3. Create model
3.3.1
Data process
The Data process window is meant to apply filters and segment data. First, the EEG-recordings
can easily be browsed, and specific files can be selected. The GUI informs how many files are
selected. At this moment, the user can choose to add an IIR notch filter at 50 Hz, and/or a Cheby-
shev type II bandpass filter from 5-50 Hz. By clicking Process data, the filters are applied on
the raw data and segmented into individual trials with the shape: [flickers×channels×samples×
sessions]. The separated files are automatically saved in a folder named Separated.
3.3.2
Model setup
The Model setup window is meant for building a SVM classification model based on the selected
segmented data sets. The user browse and select the separation files and afterwards choose the
type of features (either raw or PSD). If PSD is selected, a box to fill out sampling frequency
will appear. The model name is automatically suggested and updates if different settings are
selected. An additional text to the model name is optional. By clicking Build model, the Optuna
optimization algorithm (see details in sec. 2.7.3,6.5.1) will run a kind of effective grid search to
find the best hyperparameters in a given grid of parameters. The model is trained on a train set
through a 3-fold cross-validation and evaluated on a validation set. The results of the evaluation
are saved as a confusion matrix and other metrics in a specific folder automatically created for
the model (see Figure 3.7b).
Besides, the accuracy on the test set is displayed in the GUI.
Please note that the used data sets in Figure 3.6b were recordings on Genetic oscillations in
OpenVibe, hence the low accuracy.

Final comments on the GUI
The GUI is meant to be an easy way to design new SSVEP experiments along with highly
accurate recordings. Since this project also includes auditory stimulus, the GUI itself will not
be used during data collection from the participants, but only the actual Python code used to
create Start measurement in Figure 3.5. However, the GUI was used to verify the response of
the SSVEP stimuli, which is further described in sec. 4.3. The full code for the GUI can be
found at GUI-SSVEP-experiment-creator.
