Chapter 4
Design of the BCI
4.1
Overall design
The overall BCI interface is divided into three parts as illustrated in Fig. 4.1 (a); left, center and
right. There is only one flicker at each section, and each flicker is placed far from its neighboring
flickers. This way it is easier to focus on the flicker of interest. The layout is maintained in
all situations, so it is possible to set up an individual SSVEP and ASSR system, and a parallel
SSVEP-ASSR system. The results of each system can then be compared in the result section,
but include:
1. Test of independent SSVEP system
2. Test of independent ASSR system
3. Test of parallel SSVEP-ASSR system
The proposed BCI layout is illustrated in Figure 4.1. The entire system can be set up by simply
using a monitor for SSVEP, earphones for ASSR and an electrode cap with electrodes to record
the EEG signals. To avoid a constant (irritating) sound from the auditory system, the proposed
idea for a future BCI is to split the detection algorithm into two steps, provided that the SSVEP-
ASSR system leads to higher accuracy than the SSVEP system: 1) SSVEP detection, and 2)
SSVEP and ASSR detection. In step 1, the SSVEP detection time will be very short, but only
to indicate the predicted target of the intent, and then step 2) will act as a parallel target
verification between the visual and auditory systems. The goal is to shorten the total detection
time assumed to be achieved by this procedure.
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 4.1: Three figures (a), (b) and (c) that together illustrates a proposed BCI interface. (a)
shows the general setup of splitting the screen into three parts. (b) shows a proposed main menu,
and (c) shows an example of what might happen next after activation from the main menu.
32
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 4. Design of the BCI
4.2. Implementing a software stimuli
4.2
Implementing a software stimuli
The visual stimuli created for this project have the frequencies: 12, 20 and 30 Hz. The auditory
signals have the envelope modulation frequencies: 35, 40 and 45 Hz. Note that 40 Hz was chosen
despite being subharmonic to the 20 Hz visual stimulus. Since it is the only overlap (within the
first two harmonics), it is considered acceptable as this project is limited in the number of higher
frequencies that work for a monitor refresh rate of 60 Hz, and at the same time the auditory
signals must be as strong as possible due to the generally low ASSR accuracy. A description on
how the auditory and visual stimuli were created will now be presented.
4.2.1
ASSR stimuli
The auditory signals are generated based on inspiration from Higashi et al. [44], and as mentioned
in sec. 2.4.2, eq. 2.3, the signals are given by
s1(t) = sin(2πfct) sin(πfmt),
where t is the time course, fc is a carrier tone frequency, and fm is an envelope modulation
frequency [45].
Three different SAM signals are created for the the following spatial directions: left, right, and
both left and right. The signal from the left gets a carrier frequency of fc = 200 Hz, and an
envelope modulation frequency of fm = 35 Hz. The sampling frequency is set to fs = 4000 Hz
(must be at least twice the value of fc). For the right ear we have fc = 400 Hz and fm = 45
Hz. Finally, the audio for left and right (simultaneously) was designed to have fc = 300 Hz and
fm = 40 Hz. As in [59], these frequencies were chosen because they are around 40 Hz, where
studies have shown that humans have the strongest ASSR, but the reason is still unclear [60].
Twice as high carrier frequencies were initially tested, but a few subjects complained about the
high tone, so the tones were updated and tested on new subjects. The signals are visualized in
Figure 4.2.
0
0.05
0.1
Time (s)
-1
0
1

35 Hz AM
(a) Left
0
0.05
0.1

Figure 4.2: Three figures (a), (b) and (c), each showing the SAM signal generated for a specific
spatial direction. (a) has fc = 200 Hz and fm = 35, (b) has fc = 300 Hz and fm = 40, and (c)
has fc = 400 Hz and fm = 40 Hz. All generated with a sampling frequency of 4000 Hz.
The signals and the exported sounds are created in MATLAB R2021b. The code can be seen in
sec. F.1.1.
Page 33 of 77
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 4. Design of the BCI
4.3. Verification of SSVEP stimuli
4.2.2
SSVEP stimuli
The creation of the SSVEP stimuli was inspired by Nakanishi et al. [8] that defines a list of
frames equal to the screen’s refresh rate. A group of frames displays either the color object or
a black screen in a periodic pattern. As mentioned earlier, the number of frames must be an
integer, so only frequencies that are integer divisible of the refresh rate can be used. The stimuli
pattern is visualized in Figure 4.3 and was created in Python.
Figure 4.3: Image sequence of visual stimulation, where red indicates stimulus and dark gray is
black screen.
Ideally, the SSVEP stimuli should be fast and easy to customize or edit, and for this reason the
Psychopy package in Python would be considered a perfect Python package to use for stimulus
presentation. Psychopy can easily create new objects and be programmed to flicker at a certain
frequency and wait for the monitor to refresh. Despite this promising package, several test runs
have shown that for a 60 Hz refresh rate monitor, Psychopy returned unpredictable refresh rates
in floating values between 59-60.5 Hz tested on a MacBook Pro mid 2012 running Windows
10. For newer MacBooks (MacBook Pro 2021, M1 chip) running macOS Montery, Psychopy
was not even able to access the graphic card and returned incorrect refresh rates. For similar
reasons, the OpenBCI forums also recommend to using videos, as creating the visual stimulus
programmatically in real time can create ’jitter’ in the repeating patterns and result in other
computer delays. William J. Croft, administrator at the OpenBCI forum, states precisely:
Generating flashes programmatically in real-time, can create ’jitter’ in the frequency, be-
cause of OS, buffering, and other computer delays. Some people have had better luck
creating a video file with the flashes, because video frame rate is more tightly controlled
[61].
For this reason, the SSVEP stimuli were programmed in Python, but exported as a video file
and then displayed using VLC player. In both Windows and OS, the latest VLC version (3.1.18)
was found to cause some frames to hang and hence unstable flicker frequencies. This was fixed
by downgrading VLC to version 2.2.6.
4.3
Verification of SSVEP stimuli
The software SSVEP stimuli were tested using both OpenBCI Cyton-Daisy and g.tec USBamp
from the GUI described in chapter 3. Typically, electrodes included for SSVEP include at least:
{O1, O2, Oz} [17], [18], [19]. In ASSR, typical electrodes are: {T3,T4,T5,T6}. The electrodes
used for OpenBCI were limited to 8, so the sampling frequency would be increased to 250 Hz and
thus more comparable to g.tec, which runs at a sampling frequency of 256 Hz. The electrodes
included in OpenBCI were: {O1, O2, Pz, P3, P4, T3, T4, C3}. Note that OpenBCI does not have
an Oz electrode, which in some cases has shown the strongest signals in SSVEP measurements.
Page 34 of 77
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 4. Design of the BCI
4.3. Verification of SSVEP stimuli
Oz will be included in the g.tec setup. The electrodes included in g.tec were: {O1, O2, Oz, Pz,
P3, P4, T3, T4}.
The verification of the software stimuli includes a test of each flicker frequency with only one
flicker displayed on the screen and a test with the actual design with all three flickers on the
screen. The protocol included 1.5 s rest, 0.5 s cue target identification and 5 s stimulation. This
is equivalent to one session in the one-flicker case, while the three-flicker case would call it one
trial and repeat this procedure for all three flickers, then name it a session. A total of 5 sessions
were obtained with a 7 s break between sessions. The entire setup was performed with both green
and red stimuli, as they have shown the greatest response in SSVEP [62]. Finally, the whole
experiment for OpenBCI and g.tec was performed one after the other starting with OpenBCI. It
was carried out in a lab room with electromagnetic shielding, double doors and with the lights
off (see the room in Figure D.1).
4.3.1
g.tec
The verification test with g.tec with only one flicker on the screen is shown in Figure 4.4. A
bandpass Chebyshev type II filter, order 13, from 12-90 Hz was applied to all the time signals
together with an IIR notch filter at 50 Hz with quality factor 10. The impedance and signal
check before starting measurement revealed that the Pz electrode was the cleanest, and therefore
the plots are shown for this electrode channel.

Both Figure 4.4 and 4.5 confirm that the stimuli lead to noticeable response visualized as peaks
at the expected frequencies and up to two subharmonics. It appears to be inconsistent whether
the green or red stimulus led to the greatest response. Nevertheless, Godinez et al. [62] has
reported that the red color is less comfortable but leads to a higher ITR rate just followed by
the green color, which on the other hand is rated as more pleasant.
Furthermore, the effect of using CCA on a single trial was briefly considered. CCA takes as input
two data sets, X (nsamples, nfeatures) and Y (nsamples, ntargets). In our case, PSDs were used for
X with shape (129, 8), while a bank of expected PSDs for frequency 12, 20 and 30 Hz including
their 1st subharmonics were used as Y of shape (129, 6) (see CCA description in sec. 2.5.2). The
results can be seen in Figure 4.6.

4.3. Verification of SSVEP stimuli
The electrode channel O1 was used in Figure 4.6 over Pz to show the advantage of CCA when
the channels become a bit noisier. CCA considers all channels and returns the best correlations
to the target frequencies. The method is great especially for VEPs that have well described
characteristics.
4.3.2
OpenBCI
The same tests under the same environment and with the same filters were performed with
OpenBCI. But some strange bursts (see Figure 4.7a) occurred randomly in some trials, and
according to the OpenBCI forum it is most likely due to packet loss [63].
Packet loss can
occur when the Cyton-Daisy board wirelessly sends packets to the dongle that has established
a serial connection to the computer’s built-in FTDI chip. Packet loss can be reduced, but not
completely avoided. OpenBCI forum [63] recommends using an extension cable to bring the
dongle and Cyton-Daisy board closer to each other and turning off any other external power
source (already reduced by the lab’s electromagnetically shielded lab). The tips for reducing
noise [64] and packet loss [63] are followed for the actual data collection.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.7: Two figures (a) and (b). (a) shows an example of the bursts that occured randomly
using OpenBCI. Here in the 2nd iteration. (b) shows the averaged PSD of the EEG signals
averaged over five iterations for an SSVEP experiment with a 12 Hz flicker, either green or red.
OpenBCI at fs = 250 Hz is used as EEG acquisition.
The recording showed an accumulated noise peak around the 50 Hz frequency (and not just
the usual 50 Hz power line interference), which can be seen in Figure 4.7.
Because of this,
this project will not consider a deeper analysis in this section, but instead refer to the detailed
analysis carried out in Appendix, sec. E.1 where CCA was also used. Among others things sec.
E.1 looks at the PSD of the time signal just before the burst arrives. The amplitude and width
of the 50 Hz "peak" is much smaller, but still excessively dominates the other signals. An ideal
notch filter from 42-58 Hz was additionally applied, but Figure E.2 shows that this was also not
enough to remove it completely.

Chapter 5
Experiment
5.1
Participants
A total of 18 participants (13 male and 5 female, aged 14-60 years, and median age of 25.0)
volunteered to participate in the study.
One of them has previous experience with the BCI
system and the rest of them are naïve. Specifically for OpenBCI, 10 participants (9 male and 1
female, aged 14-29 years, and median age of 24.5) participated. For g.tec, 13 participants (8 male
and 5 female, aged 17-60 years, and median age of 25.0) participated. Another three participants
were tested on g.tec, but since these recordings ran with a different setting for marking trials,
they were decided not to be part of the later analysis. The electrode cap did not fully match one
of the subjects in OpenBCI, so this data was excluded for the later analysis. Likewise, during the
g.tec recording, a subject reported that the eyes had been closed for an uncertain amount of time.
This makes the data unpresentative, so this data set was not included in the later analysis. This
brings the total number of participants down to 9 and 12 for OpenBCI and g.tec respectively.
However, their feedback results were still used to evaluate the setup. Intentionally, the same
participants for OpenBCI were to be used in g.tec, but unfortunately not all participants could
or had time to be part of both. Nevertheless, 5 participants from the OpenBCI experiment were
part of the final g.tec setup. An overview of the collected data can be seen in A.3.
All participants were familiar with and capable of reading English. Before the experiment began,
the participant was asked to fill out an informed consent (see sec.
A.1).
The experimental
procedures were explained to the subject via both written and verbal instructions. All subjects
were asked to give feedback or report any problems during/after the experiment. The experiment
took approximately 45 minutes per subject (see the exact procedure in Figure 5.1). The subject
was placed on a comfortable chair approximately 60 cm in front of a 24-inch monitor (ASUS,
VG248QE). The time for the total experiment was distributed as seen in Figure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: A visualized overview of the time plan of the experiment.
During the experiment, the subject was asked to remain very still and move only the visual
sight of interest to reduce muscle movements and keep the electrode cables steady. Since the
electrodes from OpenBCI are passive (no pre-amplification module), shaking the electrode cables
will severely affect the signals [64]. In addition, all lights were turned off during measurements
39
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 5. Experiment
5.2. Materials and Experimental setup
and unnecessary equipment was disconnected from the power supply to limit electromagnetic
field noise in the room. In general, all advice from OpenBCI documentation forum was taken
into account before the experiments started.
5.2
Materials and Experimental setup
The materials used and the experimental setup differ slightly between the OpenBCI and g.tec
experiment, and for this reason they will be described in separate sections.
OpenBCI
As shown in Figure 5.2, the hardware system for the OpenBCI setup included: OpenBCI Cyton-
Daisy Module (sampling frequency fs = 125 Hz), OpenBCI programmable dongle, 16 x OpenBCI
dry EEG comb electrodes plus ground and reference electrode, a 24-inch ASUS VG248QE monitor
with a resolution of 1920x1080 pixels and a 60 Hz refresh rate. The monitor was connected to a
MacBook Pro (13", medio 2012, 2.9 GHz dual-core Intel Core i7-processor) using an HDMI-cable.
While the monitor was used to present the visual stimuli, a SONY WH-XB900N headset was
used to present the auditory stimuli, but also to create a closer contact between the dry electrode
and the scalp. Furthermore, the impedance of each channel was checked with the OpenBCI GUI
version 5.1.0 before the experiment started. The location of each electrode is shown in Figure
5.2a. The electrodes, {O1, O2, Pz, P3, P4}, are of particular interest for the SSVEP experiment
[17], [18], [15], and {T3 (T7), T4 (T8)} for the ASSR experiment [19]. The experiment was
carried out in a quiet meeting room at DTU in order to imitate a home-like setup as best as
possible. A green visual stimulus color was used to prioritize a combination of high comfort and
accuracy [62].
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 5.2: Three figures (a), (b) and (c). (a) shows the active electrodes in the OpenBCI setup,
where the ground and reference electrode are marked with yellow and blue colors, respectively.
The red colors indicate the actual electrodes of interest, while the gray colors indicate the re-
maining active electrodes that were included to easily share the device with others using the
device during the same time period (adapted from [65]). (b) shows the OpenBCI hardware used

Chapter 5. Experiment
5.2. Materials and Experimental setup
g.tec
A few changes were made for the g.tec setup. First of all, the experiment took place in a elec-
tromagnetic shielded room (DTU building 354, Physiology lab 2) and the device used was g.tec
USBamp 16 channel amplifier (sampling frequency fs = 256 Hz) including the g.GAMMAbox
and 8 x g.LADYBIRD active EEG electrodes. Conductive gel was added to the electrodes, the
ground electrode was moved to FpZ, and the reference electrode was moved to the ear lobe.
Because of the new position of the reference electrode, the headset was replaced by Galaxy S8
InEar headphones (EO-IG955BSEGW tuned by AKG). Otherwise, the materials also included a
24-inch ASUS VG248QE monitor with a resolution of 1920x1080 pixels and a 60 Hz refresh rate,
connected to a MacBook Pro (13", medio 2012, 2.9 GHz dual-core Intel Core i7-processor) using
an HDMI-cable. The impedance levels for each electrode channel were checked with OpenVibe
Acquisition Server using g.tec USBamp Legacy as driver (see details in sec. 6). A visualization
of the g.tec setup and the electrode positions can be seen in Figure 5.3. The electrodes, {O1, O2,
Oz, Pz, P3, P4}, are of particular interest for the SSVEP experiment [17], [18], [15], and {T3
(T7), T4 (T8)} for the ASSR experiment [19]. Additional pictures can be seen in sec. D.1.
It is important to note that the OpenBCI experiments were performed before g.tec. The discus-
sion in sec. 8 will comment on some of the issues and low signal quality found with OpenBCI.
The OpenBCI signals were of such a low quality that it was decided to improve the signal quality
by placing the reference electrode at the earlobe and using a red visual stimulus instead of green
to use a color that supposedly led to stronger response [62].
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 5.3: Three figures (a), (b) and (c). (a) shows the active electrodes in the g.tec setup, where
the ground and reference electrodes are marked by the yellow and blue colors, respectively. The
red colors indicate the active electrodes (from [65] but modified). (b) shows the g.tec hardware
used, and (c) shows the actual setup and materials.
SSVEP data collection
Figure 5.4: The SSVEP protocol.
The SSVEP part of the experiment and protocol will draw
inspiration from the proposed setup by Ge et al. [67]. A to-
tal of three flickers are displayed on the monitor at all times
as presented in the interface design in chapter 4.
As illustrated in Fig. 5.4, a trial consists of a rest time
(1.5 s), cue time (0.5 s) and a SSVEP stimulation time (5
s). During the cue target time, a square on one of the three flickers would indicate the next
target stimulus, and the subject would be required to shift gaze to the indicated target stimulus
Page 41 of 77
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 5. Experiment
5.2. Materials and Experimental setup
as quickly as possible and keep concentrating on it. During the target stimulation, the visual
stimuli will be presented. There are three trials in a session corresponding to the number of
flickers. The target stimuli in each session are shown once and in random order. A total of
20 sessions will be carried out with a 7 s rest period between sessions, therefore the SSVEP
experiment time will be:
[(1.5 + 0.5 + 5) × 3 + 7]s × 20 = 560 s,
so approximately 10 minutes.
ASSR data collection
Figure 5.5: The ASSR protocol.
The ASSR part of the experiment and protocol have
drawn inspiration from [59]. Two spatial directions (left
and right) and three different auditory stimuli were used
with a SAM frequency of 35 Hz (for left), 40 Hz (left and
right) and 45 Hz (for right).
A trial in the ASSR experiment consists of three stimuli (of 3 s each) and three rest times (of
0.375 s each). This is repeated 10 times, defining one session. During these 10 times, the user is
asked to only concentrate on the target from one side (see Figure 5.5). All other stimuli should
be ignored and considered as non-targets. To cover all three target stimuli, the procedure is
repeated 3 times with a 20 s rest in between. 3 s stimulation time instead of 5 s was chosen
based on a study by [45], where 5 s long auditory stimulation time was found to increase fatigue
and sleepiness and caused a decrease in interface efficiency. The aim is also to consider a BCI
that can reduce the detection time of SSVEP, not increase it.
Before the experiment, the subject was presented with left, right and both auditory stimuli to
learn and memorize the different auditory stimuli and was also asked to adjust the base volume
to the volume with which they were comfortable. Subjects were asked to sit comfortably and put
on the earphones/headphones. The subjects were further asked to keep their eyes open during
the experiment and to look at an arrow in the center of the monitor, indicating the listening
direction. Overall, this leads to an ASSR experiment time of
[(0.375 + 3) × 3 × 10 + 20]s × 3 = 364 s,
so approximately 6 minutes.
SSVEP-ASSR data collection
The SSVEP-ASSR data collection will follow the same protocol as made for the ASSR protocol
(see Figure 5.5), but the experiment itself is a combination of SSVEP and ASSR. Once again, the
subject must listen to one channel direction for 10 trials, starting with the left, but in this case
the subject must also focus on the left visual stimulus at all times, even if the auditory stimulus
changes direction. After the 10 trials, the subject is asked to listen to the sound coming to both
ears AND focus on the flicker in the middle. In the last 10 trials, the subject must listen only to
the sound from the right while looking only at the flicker on the right of the screen.
Page 42 of 77
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 6
Methods used
The previous chapters contained a description of the general theory, BCI design and experimental
information. This chapter will go into the methodology of the data collection scripts, the data
processing, the selection of features, and finally the classification algorithms. The purpose of this
work is to prepare for a simple BCI. The computational load is therefore a concern even though
the data analysis is done offline.
6.1
Data collection scripts
The EEG acquisition devices included OpenBCI Cyton-Daisy Module and g.tec USBamp. Access
to OpenBCI was granted from day one of this project, whereas access to g.tec was obtained at a
later stage of the project. For this reason, OpenBCI was the first acquisition device to be set up
and to provide the necessary code for. Once connection to the acquisition device was established,
the Python script was executed to run and open the GUI (see Figure 6.1).
43
==PAGE:58==
Chapter 6. Methods used
6.1. Data collection scripts
Figure 6.1: Data collection GUI for SSVEP, ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR, which in the upper
image shows the pop-up that occurs after clicking on Start measurement. The second image
shows a brief instruction of the experiment about to begin. A similar window appears between
each session. Parts of the GUI is based on code developed by Jonas Vishart in the special course
"Design and construction of an interface for EEG-based BCI, including the creation of a protocol
for the EEG procedure".
User and project ID information was filled in before starting the EEG recording, which was
used to name the data file properly. The data file contained information about when a trial
was started and finished.
Next, the actual measurement starts, following the EEG protocol
described in section 5.2.
The data acquisition for OpenBCI and g.tec will be described in
separate sections.
6.1.1
OpenBCI - Brainflow and Python
The OpenBCI device was used in conjunction with Python and the Brainflow package [68] in
Python. Brainflow is optimal for BCI development as connection can be established directly from
Python (using the OpenBCI dongle inserted to the PC) to the acquisition board (Cyton-Daisy
board). The code below shows how the port and board were defined in Python:
1 #Define
parameters
( port and board )
2 params = BrainFlowInputParams ()
3 params . serial_port = "/dev/cu . u sbs e r i a l −DM0258IX"
4 board = BoardShim ( BoardIds .CYTON_DAISY_BOARD. value ,
params )
Hence, data acquisition can be started and stopped in Python, and filtered signals can be dis-
played live in a window. If the port is not automatically found, sec. E.2 shows how to get the
serial number of the port. A tutorial from [69] was used to get started using OpenBCI Cyton-
Daisy. The start and end of a trial was easily added in a separate column in the saved recording
using board.insert_marker() in the Brainflow package. After the acquisition, the data were
segmented into trials based on the trial markers (see code in sec. F.2.1).
Page 44 of 77
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 6. Methods used
6.1. Data collection scripts
6.1.2
g.tec - OpenVibe and Python
Brainflow is not compatible with g.tec and therefore OpenVibe has been used. OpenVibe includes
several drivers that make it compatible with e.g. g.tec USBamp, OpenBCI and LSL (lab stream-
ing layer). Unfortunately, OpenVibe only works on Windows and Linux operating systems. A
major update from the Brainflow package to OpenVibe includes trials marking. Previously, the
start and end of a trial were marked in a separate column using Brainflow, which can be done
in a similar way using TCP-tagging that sends information to the OpenVibe acquisition server.
In that case, both OpenVibe Designer, OpenVibe Acquisition Server and Python will be needed.
If LSL is used as an alternative to OpenVibe Designer, it will not be possible to store Event
IDs and Event Dates, but only the samples for each channel. Although this may be sufficient,
as LSL can also send data marks, all three programs (OpenVibe Acquisition Server, OpenVibe
Designer and Python) will be used, since the user is recommended to check the signal quality no
matter what, which cannot be done in the acquisition server. The only accessible g.tec driver
provided for this project is g.tec USBamp Legacy driver which is a legacy driver developed for
g.tec USBamp devices. It is not officially supported anymore. Nevertheless, several test runs
with OpenVibe 3.4.0 and the g.tec USBamp driver have shown that it works as expected with
the g.tec USBamp device. More details on the TCP tagging will now be presented.
TCP tagging in OpenVibe
TCP tagging is a technique for sending labels to the OpenVibe Acquisition server from external
software, in this case Python. Python presents the stimuli and sends a TCP tag just before and
after a trial. OpenVibe has predefined tag labels that can be found in [70]. For this project,
the value 0x00008105 indicates the start of a trial while 0x00008106 indicates the end. Below is
attached an example of how TCP tagging is done in this project:

It is very important that the PORT information matches the PORT listed in OpenVibe Acquisition
server. Also, the EVENT_IDs must be in byte format and send together with a timestamp. Without
the timestamp, the correct number of tags will not be received.
Furthermore, exact clock hours of stimuli presentation are exported from Python as a csv file
to compare with the measurement recording in OpenVibe. Drift and jitter might happen dur-
ing recording. The drift and jitter have been reduced as much as possible by setting Drift
Correction to Force and limiting the Drift Tolerance (ms) to 3 ms. Lower tolerances still
result in the same drift and jitter. After producing the tags, the next step will be to find the tags
in the exported csv file. Even if the actual tag is known, OpenVibe might send the tag either as
a string or an integer that changes randomly between experiments. A script is created to find
the correct tags and separate the data into individual trials (see sec. F.2.1).
6.2
Data preprocessing
Before any signal preprocessing, data preprocessing is performed. This includes handling both
missing/lost data and imbalanced data.
6.2.1
Handling missing or lost data
TCP tagging not only ensures that each trial matches the execution of stimulus, but also reveals
if any data is lost due to drift. The actual stimulus duration is given as the time difference
between the start and stop tag. Usually the actual duration is longer than the expected duration
due to various delays. After the expected duration, the state of the video is checked and if it is
Page 46 of 77
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 6. Methods used
6.2. Data preprocessing
still playing, 50 ms is added to the recording. The video itself is not delayed, but only its start
time. However, if drift occurs, some data will be lost and the actual stimulus duration will be
shorter than the expected length. In such cases, a typical solution is either zero-padding or mean
values to the beginning of the recording. The result of this is seen in Figure 6.2a. There are two
main reasons behind this: 1) to maintain the data structure, and 2) the PSD features will be
minimal affected by zero-padding. Ideally, it would be best to drop that trial as no information
is given on when the drift happened, but this would lead to an imbalanced data frame structure
that is incomparable between subjects. Data is important, so it is not of interest to cut all
the other trials to the minimum duration. This project has decided not to do interpolation or
padding, but instead create the PSD based on the actual signal. The assumption for this is that
there is always at least 2 s of valid data in each trial. In that case, the PSD can be set to always
return values equal half the sampling frequency, thereby keeping the dimension regardless the
input size.
(a)
(b)
Figure 6.2: Two figures (a) and (b), both showing the PSD of the Oz time signal for subject
3, 20 Hz flicker at session 11. Due to drift, 0.60 s of the expected duration (7 s) was lost. (a)
shows two examples of what will happen to the signal if the missing values are replaced by either
zeros or the mean at the beginning of the trial. The result of zero-padding is very identical to
mean-padding in this case, which is why the blue curve is hidden behind the orange curve. (b)
is the PSD of the valid data only.
Although the two plots in Figure 6.2 appear very similar, there are still a few important differ-
ences, such as the 24 Hz peak that appears a slightly sharper in 6.2b compared to 6.2a. Therefore,
the PSD transform will only be performed on actual data and skipping nan values.
6.2.2
Handling imbalanced data
The setup of the ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR experiments lead to an imbalanced data set of generally
four classes: [True left (listen left and sound from left); True middle (listen to both sides and
sound to both ears); True right (listen right and sound from right); False]. False accounts for
multiple situations and is the majority class, which can make the model tend to predict False.
To correct for the imbalanced data set in ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR, the data set is randomly
undersampled to the minority class. The distribution before and after can be seen in Figure
6.3.
Page 47 of 77
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 6. Methods used
6.3. Signal preprocessing
(a)
(b)
Figure 6.3: Two figures (a) and (b) showing the class distribution of the ASSR experiment before
and after the correction of the imbalanced data set for one subject, respectively. Note that 0
stands for True left, 1 for True middle, 2 for True right, and 3 for False.
6.3
Signal preprocessing
In sec. 2.5 it was mentioned that the signal preprocessing can be divided into filtering, artifact
removal and segmentation. Only filters that can be used for online analysis (causal filters) are
used. It is important to note that the filters in 6.3.1 were applied before data segmentation. The
artifact removal of using either CCA or CAR or both or none were evaluated in sec. 6.4.2. That
step is performed after data segmentation.
6.3.1
Filters applied
The filters applied on the data sets obtained using OpenBCI are selected so the signals outside
f0 ± width are not reduced more than maximum 5%. Two IIR notch filters are applied, the
traditional at f0 = 50 Hz with a quality factor (Q) of 10, and another at f0 = 25 Hz with
Q = 30. The extra 25 Hz notch filter was necessary as OpenBCI produces this extra peak when
the sampling frequency is 125 Hz. Figure 6.4 shows the applied filters. An example of the signals
after application of filters are shown in sec. E.3.
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 6.4: Three figures (a), (b) and (c) showing the filters applied on the data sets obtained
using the OpenBCI device. (a) is a 50 Hz notch filter with Q = 10. (b) is a 25 Hz notch filter
with Q = 30. (c) is a Chebyshev type II high-pass filter of order 11, width=2.5 Hz, low-cut =
12 Hz, pass-band ripple of 0.4 dB, and a stop-band attenuation of 50 dB.

==PAGE:64==
Chapter 6. Methods used
6.4. Feature extraction
6.4
Feature extraction
Sec. 2.6 highlighted some of the common feature extractions used in BCI applications. PSD and
CCA were particularly useful for VEP-based BCIs as described in sec. 1. Feature extraction
is an important step when using a machine learning model like SVM. The CNN model, on the
other hand, performs automatic feature extraction, so the step is less relevant. Nevertheless, the
same features will be used for SVM and CNN to make a fair comparison. The feature extraction
process has been divided into two blocks to 1) find best data input evaluated on the g.tec data
for each of the three experiment types, ASSR, SSVEP and SSVEP-ASSR, and 2) find the best
combination of electrode channels to include for the ASSR, SSVEP and SSVEP-ASSR system
individually. The workflow is illustrated in Figure 6.7.
Figure 6.7: Overview of the feature extraction and classification approach for this project.
6.4.1
Train-test split
There are two common training scenarios for BCI applications: user-dependent (UD) and user-
independent (UI). In a user-dependent training scenario, the classifier is trained and tested on
the same user. The classifier in a user-independent scenario is trained across all subjects and
tested on a new test user. SSVEP-based BCIs have potential to be a generalized model (a UI
model), which is favorable because it makes a training-free method with no calibration needed
for the unseen user [71].
This project will consider both scenarios for the analysis section. The hyperparameters for the
SVM and CNN model will be based on the highest accuracy on the validation set across all
selected participants and used in both the UI and UD scenario. The train-test splitting ratio is
70:30. The test set is meant to only be considered once. 30% of the train set will be used as
validation in the CNN model for hyperparameter tuning, while a 3-fold cross-validation is used
during training of the SVM model. The validation set metrics will be checked multiple times.
For this reason, the validation set must have a descent size, especially for smaller data sets, to
avoid overfitting on a small validation set.
In the UD scenario, the train-test splitting ratio is 70:30 for each subject, where 30% of the train
set will be used as validation set in the CNN model to define the internal hyperparameters and
do automatic feature extraction. The UI scenario will use subject 2 as test set and all other
subjects for training (see details on model input in sec. 7.1).
Before the UD and UI analysis, the initial steps to decide on the best data input, the best
channels, the best optimizer algorithm, and the optimal hyperparameters for both the SVM and
CNN model will be evaluated across all subjects with more details in the following sections.
Page 50 of 77
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 6. Methods used
6.4. Feature extraction
6.4.2
Best data input
To conclude which extraction methods should be included as input to the classification algo-
rithms, a statistical comparison of the performance was made on the g.tec data for all the
experiment types was performed. A model for each of the following data input was created:
CCA, CCA with CAR, PSD, PSD with CAR, time signals, and time signals with CAR. Since all
observations include the same signals with different operations applied, we have to account for
the non-independence of samples problem. This can be done through a corrected paired t-test
(also known as Diebold-Mariano test):
t =
1
k·r
Pk
i=1
Pr
j=1 xij
r

k·r + ntest
ntrain

ˆσ2
,
(6.1)
where k is the number of folds in the cross-validation, r is the number of repetitions in the cross-
validation, x is the difference in the performance of the models, ntest is the number of samples
used for testing, ntrain is the number of samples used for training, and ˆσ2 is the variance of the
observed differences [72].
Depending on the results, the p-values may be above the significance level of α = 0.05. If so, it
cannot be rejected that one model is better than the other. For this reason, Bayesian estimation
suggested by Benavoli and colleagues [73] is used to compare the models. The models use a SVM
classifier with an automatic value for γ. It is trained with a cross-validation of 10 folds and 10
repetitions on the training data to get enough values to make a proper statistical evaluation.
Note that the data set is ini tially split into a train and a test set (ratio 70:30). The results can
be seen in Table 6.1 (see the code in sec. F.2.2). Note the same analysis was done for ASSR
and SSVEP-ASSR to ensure that the conclusion was the same for all of them. Their results are
shown in Table D.1 and D.2. ASSR gave results close to random, so those results will not be
part of the best data input conclusion.

The statistical test shows that CCA as input data leads to the best classification results. The
high p-value (0.78) concludes that the null hypothesis cannot be rejected, and therefore it is
only by chance that CCA as input performs better than CCA with CAR as input. However, the
Bayesian results conclude that there is an 89.57% probability that CCA as input performs better
than using CCA with CAR as input. CCA is clearly better than PSD as input seen by p = 0.00.

6.4. Feature extraction
For this reason and taking into account the additional computation time from including CAR,
CCA will be used as input in the later models. Also note that in most other cases the p-value
is 0, indicating that the model M1 is significantly better than model M2 at a significance level of
α = 0.05.
6.4.3
Best channels
It is possible to combine the 8 electrodes in 255 ways. However, this project will only consider
three combinations for g.tec: {T3, T4} (auditory), {O1, O2, Oz, Pz, P3, P4} (visual), {O1, O2,
Oz, Pz, P3, P4, T3, T4} (both). Similarly, for OpenBCI: {T3, T4} (auditory), {O1, O2, Pz,
P3, P4} (visual), {O1, O2, Pz, P3, P4, T3, T4} (both). Based on the previous result, we only
consider the CCA input.

Conclusion on best channels
Table 6.2 and 6.3 show more or less the expected result that using only electrodes positioned
at the cortex for the expected response leads to better results. This is the case in the SSVEP
experiment where the best accuracy is 67.02% ± 6.93% using the electrodes at the visual cortex.
Page 52 of 77
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 6. Methods used
6.5. Classification algorithms
Note the p-value is 0.13, hence above the significance level of α = 0.05, and therefore it is not
significant. Table 6.3, on the other hand, shows accuracy at chance level. Both the p-value of 1.00
and the probabilities of obtaining a worse, better or equal accuracy when using all electrodes
indicate that it is impossible to conclude whether it is better to use just the auditory or all
electrodes. Finally, Table 6.4 achieves its highest accuracy of 41.09% ± 7.28% when using the
electrodes at the visual cortex, but it does not lead to a significantly better result than when
using all electrodes.
In the later analysis, all electrodes will be used as the statistical results could not show a signifi-
cant difference from using all electrodes. Instead more electrodes give the CNN model a broader
options of features to extract (in the CNN model) and increase the redundancy. CCA is used as
input (between PSD of all the EEG signals and PSD of the stimuli frequencies specific for the
experiment).
6.5
Classification algorithms
Optimization usually refers to finding the best hyperparameters for the classification algorithm.
To find these hyperparameters, an optimizer is used. Various optimizers will now be consid-
ered.
6.5.1
Optimizer
Three different optimizers (grid search, genetic algorithm and Optuna) will be evaluated on the
SSVEP data set from g.tec to decide on which optimizer to use for the classification analysis.
Accuracy and computational time are of most importance. The results are shown in Table 6.5,
6.6 and 6.7.

Page 55 of 77
==PAGE:70==
Chapter 6. Methods used
6.5. Classification algorithms
6.5.3
Neural network
A simple CNN structure is proposed to avoid overfitting of data, especially when CCA is used
as input. The model structure is inspired by Ravi et al. [71] who performed classification on a
SSVEP data set via CNN.
This project will use a convolutional input layer, a convolutional hidden layer, a fully connected
layer and an output layer (3 for SSVEP, and 4 for ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR). A dropout is added
after each layer to avoid overfitting on training set. No batch normalization or max pooling will
be used as the CNN architecture is relatively simple and the size of the data does not need to
be scaled down to any great extent.
Figure 6.9: An illustration of the used CNN architecture. Inspiration from [71].
The optimal number of neurons and dropout rates are determined through the Optuna optimiza-
tion. The final hyperparameters to tune include the learning rate of the Adam optimizer and
the batch size. The number of epochs is set to 300 at all times, but if the validation loss is not
improved within 10 epochs (early stopping), the model will stop training on more epochs. The
exact model input and hyperparameter search space are defined in Table 6.10.
Model input and info
Experiment:
SSVEP, ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR
Device:
g.tec and OpenBCI
Electrode channels:
g.tec: {O1,O2,Oz,Pz,P3,P4,T3,T4}
OpenBCI: {C3,O2,O1,Pz,P4,P3,T4,T3}
Subjects incl.:
g.tec: {S1,S2,S3,S4,S5,S7,S8,S9,S10,S11,S12,S13}
OpenBCI: {S1,S2,S3,S4,S5,S6,S7,S8,S9}
Segmentation length:
SSVEP: 5 s. ASSR: 3 s. SSVEP-ASSR: 3 s
Features:
CCA between PSD of EEG data and PSD of: f = {12,20,30} Hz for SSVEP, f =
{35,40,45} Hz for ASSR, and f={12,20,30,35,40,45} Hz for SSVEP-ASSR, incl. one
subharmonic
Classification:
CNN
Optimization:
Optuna
Parameters constant:
Activation: ReLU. Number of trials: 100. Epochs: 300. Early stopping
acceptance: 10 epochs
Parameters tuned:
Filter(s) ∈{8, 16, 32}. Neurons flatten layer ∈{6, 8, 10, 12, 14, 16}.
Kernel size(s) ∈[1..3]. Dropout rate(s) ∈{0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.4}.
Learning rate ∈[1 × 10−5, 1 × 10−2]. Batch size ∈{32, 64}.
Table 6.10: Hyperparameter tuning of the CNN classifier on the SSVEP data set from g.tec
using Optuna as optimization algorithm. Note the model itself uses the Adam optimizer.

Chapter 6. Methods used
6.5. Classification algorithms
6.5.4
Performance evaluation metrics
The performance of the classifiers will be evaluated on not only the accuracy, but also precision,
recall, specificity and F1-score. The metric will be obtained from the confusion matrix on the
final test set (in a 3-fold split of the test set). The expressions for each metric are given below and
depends on the true positives (TP), true negatives (TN), false positives (FP), and false negative
(FN) values:
Accuracy =
TP + TN
TP + TN + FP + FN,
Precision =
TP
TP + FP,
Recall =
TP
TP + FN,
Specificity =
TN
TN + FP,
F1-score = 2 × Precision × Recall
Precision + Recall
.
(6.2)
To make the metrics usable in multiclass models, a "One-vs-the-Rest (OvR)" method is used.
In that case, TP, TN, FP and FN will become as illustrated in Figure 6.12.
Figure 6.12: An example of indication of True positive (TP), True negative (TN), False positive
(FP) and False negative (FN) for class C1 in a four class confusion matrix. The green font
highlights the cases where the predicted class matches the actual class. The red boxes show the
FNs and FPs for class C1.
As can be seen in eq. 6.2 and the example in Figure 6.12, accuracy is simply the percentage of
correct predictions made by the classifier. The precision indicates the actual true samples out of
the samples that were predicted to be true. Recall is a measure of how many of the true samples
were correctly classified as true. Specificity is kind of the opposite of precision, it talks about
the number of non-targets correctly predicted. Finally, the F1-score is a way to combine recall
and precision into a single metric and weight them equally. It makes it suitable for imbalanced
data sets, as the F1-score takes the error type (FP or FN) into account. Note that the confusion
matrices in chapter 7 are normalized row-wise so that each diagonal value corresponds to the
recall of each class.
Page 59 of 77
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 7
Results
This chapter presents the results of the g.tec and OpenBCI experiments using the methods de-
scribed in previous chapter. This includes first of all, the SVM and CNN classification algorithms
found and designed (in terms of hyperparameters) through the Optuna optimization. The re-
sults are evaluated on an unseen subject (S2) and on each subject individually (UD). Next, an
analysis of the segment lengths will be made, followed by an analysis of using just the O2 and
T4 electrodes. Finally, an overview of the feedback results will be presented.
7.1
Test on unseen subject (UI)
The model performances are evaluated on subject 2 as a test set. The section is divided into a
section for g.tec and OpenBCI to view the results of the classifiers separately.

7.4
Single electrode analysis
It is of interest to reduce the number of electrodes both in terms of cost and convenience.
Here, the UNEEG Medical implant is put into perspective to see if the device can be used for
SSVEP-based BCIs. The implant itself consists of three 1 cm electrodes (leads) to form two
bipolar channels (DSQ-CSQ and PSQ-CSQ), as the center electrode is used for reference. The
housing contains an inductive coil for transfer of power and data. The implant and its position
is illustrated in Figure 7.7.
(a) UNEEG Medical implant
(b) Position of UNEEG Medical implant
Figure 7.7: Illustration of the UNEEG Medical A/S implant (a) and position (b). A indicates
the housing, B the isolated wire, and C is the 1 cm center electrode/lead (CSQ) used for reference.
The lead closest to the housing (proximal) is labeled PSQ while the distal electrode is labeled
DSQ. Copyright ©UNEEG Medical A/S.
The implant is positioned somewhere between F8-T4, but the exact position of the implant is
determined during the surgical procedure by the surgeon and the epileptologist [74]. For that
reason, this project will now consider the results if only the T4 electrode was used as input and
compare it to the results of using only the O2 electrode (closest electrode from the occipital
lobe). Note that an input of just one electrode greatly reduces the dimensionality, and therefore
the 1D convolutional layers in the CNN architecture will be replaced by a dense layer. More
information about model input can be seen in Table 7.12. The best hyperparameters for each
feature type and for each electrode were found across all subjects using Optuna as an optimizer
and evaluated on a validation set (30% of training set). The found hyperparameters are listed
in Table 7.13.

Page 69 of 77
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 8
Discussion
This chapter will look into, interpret and evaluate the research findings from chapter 7. The
results, which included tests on an unseen (UI) and on each (UD) subject, will be presented and
discussed together but for g.tec and OpenBCI separately. The findings will be compared with
the feedback results and held up against the literature review of this topic. The findings from
g.tec will include the segmentation length and single electrode input results. All the findings
will be used to answer this thesis’ research questions, including a description on future research
perspectives.
8.1
g.tec findings
SSVEP
The overall accuracy in the UI scenario using the CNN and SVM classifier for the SSVEP
experiment was found to be 86.67±4.71% and 85.00±4.08%, respectively. The F1-score provides
a quick evaluation of the correctly classified samples of each class and was found to be between
77.78±7.86% to 97.78±3.14% in the SSVEP experiment using g.tec and the CNN classifier. If the
SVM classifier was used, the F1-scores ranged between 72.21±22.87% and 100.00±0.00%.
The F1-scores for class C1 (30 Hz flicker) were 82.74±5.52% and 80.52±13.11% using CNN and
SVM as a classifier, respectively. These results and the stimulus verification in sec. 4.3 shows
that higher stimulus frequencies can be detected, which is particularly interesting for designing
a BCI that does not trigger epileptic seizures (if the frequency is above 25 Hz). The EEG PSD
amplitude response to the 30 Hz flicker (see Figure 4.5) was lower than to the 12 and 20 Hz
flicker, indicating that higher frequencies may have even lower amplitudes and may be more
difficult to detect. Similarly, Anindya et al. [19] got a lower accuracy (71.67%) for a data set
with stimulus frequencies of 8, 14 and 28 Hz, compared to a data set with 6-12 Hz flickers, where
the accuracy was 83.26%. As seen in Figure 7.9 (a) and (b), high frequency stimulation (30 Hz)
was also the most comfortable. An investigation into how to further improve comfort would be
interesting.
The high accuracies in the UI scenario confirms the finding from Ravi et al. [71] that SSVEP-
based BCIs can be used in generalized models. The UD scenario also led to a high average
accuracy of 84.19 ± 11.32%, despite including subject 1 and 6 that resulted in an accuracy of
50.00 ± 27.22% and 61.11 ± 15.71%, respectively. On the other hand, subject 4 resulted in an
accuracy of 100.00 ± 0.00% regardless of the classifier (CNN or SVM). A comparison between
the PSD signals (averaged over iterations and channels) for subject 1 and 4 is made to look at
why the classification accuracy was worse for subject 1 (see Figure 8.1).
70
==PAGE:85==
Chapter 8. Discussion
8.1. g.tec findings
(a) SSVEP
(b) SSVEP
Figure 8.1: A comparison between the PSD signals in the SSVEP experiment with g.tec (averaged
over iterations and channels) for subject 1 and subject 4. The average PSD for the other flickers
(of subject 4) can be seen in Figure D.4.
Figure 8.1 shows easily detectable peaks for subject 4, which is not the case for subject 1. Why
the signal to noise ratio seems to be reduced for subject 1 is difficult to tell. One explanation
could be that the impedance levels were too high, but as mentioned in sec. 5, the impedance
levels were checked for all subjects before the start of the measurement (see the impedance level
for subject 1 in Figure D.2). The signals were also considered by the investigator throughout
the measurement. Despite the acceptable impedance levels and overall acceptable signals during
the measurement, the outcome was of low condition. It took some time to place the electrodes
correctly on the subject, so replacing the conductive gel on the reference electrode could have
improved the results. There is also a risk that the response to the stimuli is unclear for that
subject.
ASSR
The overall accuracy of the ASSR experiment in the UI setup was found to be just 37.73±7.20%
and 24.73 ± 12.21% using the CNN and SVM classifier, respectively. Hence, the accuracies are
not far from 25%, which would be chance level. The user-dependent scenario led to slightly
better average results: 40.38 ± 25.94% and 37.82 ± 20.67% using the CNN and SVM classifier,
respectively. Nevertheless, none of the ASSR results are at a level where it would make sense to
set up a ASSR-based BCI.
By looking at the feedback results given in sec. A.2, the majority believe it was difficult to
focus on just one sound. It was given a mean score of 7.64 ± 2.14 between 1-10, with 10 being
considered very difficult. This is reflected in the low accuracies. It could be considered whether
a different type of sound would have improved the results. There was no consensus among the
subjects as to whether the sounds were difficult to distinguish from each other, approximately
half of the subjects ranged it between 1-5 and the other half between 6-10, leading to a mean
value of 5.06 ± 2.62 where 10 is considered very difficult.
The study from Punsawad et al. [15] got an accuracy score of 81±5.18% with the speaker placed
in front of subject. Hence, a speaker instead of earphones and at a different position, may have
led to better results. Also Punsawad et al. noted that a training session was done beforehand,
otherwise the results were 59 ± 9.34% in a 4 class problem (baseline, both sides, left side, and
right side). It is also possible that the low accuracies were due to more noise at the temporal
area. To investigate this, the average PSD amplitudes averaged over iterations and over the
channel groups T3,T4 (auditory cortex) and O1,O2,Oz,Pz,P3,P4,T3,T4 (all) are shown. The
Page 71 of 77
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 8. Discussion
8.1. g.tec findings
amplitudes can be seen in Figure 8.2.
(a) ASSR
(b) ASSR
Figure 8.2: A comparison between the PSD signals from subject 4 in the ASSR experiment with
g.tec (averaged over iterations and channels) between using only the auditory electrodes (T3,
T4) or all electrodes (O1,O2,Oz,Pz,P3,P4,T3,T4). The average PSD for the other targets can
be seen in Figure D.5.
Figure 8.2 shows that the average amplitude is higher but not extreme for the electrodes at
the primary auditory cortex (T3, T4). A less noisy position at the temporal cortex could have
been chosen, but then it would have been further away from the primary auditory region. More
electrodes increase the redundancy, but too many electrodes are not of interest for a BCI for
home use and would also require a longer setup time. On the other hand, the results from Figure
7.8 (d) shows that using only the T4 electrode actually achieves an accuracy of 59.26 ± 8.00% in
the SSVEP experiment, indicating that the classes in the ASSR experiment were more difficult
to classify. Either because the task was simply too difficult for the user, or because the stimuli
did not produce a strong ASSR response at the expected frequencies. If g.tec would have been
available earlier in this project, there would have been time to verify the response to the auditory
stimuli as was done with the visual stimuli in sec. 4.3.
SSVEP-ASSR
The hybrid SSVEP-ASSR-based setup did also not manage to overcome the classification results
of the SSVEP-based setup. In fact the F1-scores in the UI scenario ranged between 0.00±0.00%
and 83.33±11.79% using CNN as classifier and between 13.33±18.86% and 95.24±6.73% using
the SVM model as classifier. In both cases, the C2 class (right) achieved the highest F1-scores,
while the false class was very low for both classifiers. Instead of predicting the true class False,
both models tended to predict it as class Middle instead. There is therefore no indication that
the hybrid SSVEP-ASSR-based setup will shorten the detection time.
Figure 7.6 and Table 7.11 did reveal that CCA as input can reduce the segmentation length to
3.5 s and still achieved an accuracy of 86.42 ± 1.75%. Therefore, additional signal processing
techniques are likely to lead to higher accuracies, but also increase the computation time and
slow down the BCI. An ideal model would require minimal processing (as close to raw signals as
possible) and still predict with high accuracy. In perspective, a 3.5 s detection time for smart
home activation may be fast compared to voice activation, but not for sentence writing.
Classifier
The CNN classifier resulted in better overall and average accuracies in both the UI and UD
scenario and for each experiment type (SSVEP, ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR). The CNN classifier
Page 72 of 77
==PAGE:87==
Chapter 8. Discussion
8.2. OpenBCI findings
therefore appears to be more optimal as a classification algorithm than the SVM classifier in this
kind of setup. However, note that the accuracy differences between CNN and SVM turned out to
be very small. The preliminary study done in Table 6.1 (before optimization) shows that SVM
achieves a better result using CCA over PSD, an accuracy of 71.7±5.76% vs. 63.41±7.09%. SVM
is highly dependent on the feature extractions, whereas Table 7.11 shows that the CNN model
achieves almost identical and high accuracies regardless of whether it is PSD or CCA that is used
as input as long as the model has enough data to select features from during training.
8.2
OpenBCI findings
SSVEP, ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR
The verification of SSVEP stimuli in sec. 4.3.2 and E.1 indicated that it would be difficult to
detect SSVEP response using the OpenBCI device with the head cap. Nevertheless, the project
investigated whether a CNN network would be able to extract relevant features despite the noise
in the signals and in the office room environment. The results in chapter 7 conclude that it was
not possible. The overall accuracy for the SSVEP experiment in the UI scenario and the average
accuracy in the UD scenario ranged between 31.67±17.00% and 33.33±2.36%, which chance level
for a three-class classification problem. Similar results were obtained for the ASSR and SSVEP-
ASSR experiment, where the overall and average accuracies ranged between 24.91 ± 6.79% and
28.70 ± 20.79%. The confusion matrices in Figure 7.3 and Figure 7.4 all show that both the
CNN and SVM models tend to only predict the same class, but not the same class across the
confusion matrices.
In addition to the 50 Hz power line frequency that typically appear in EEG measurements, a 25
Hz peak was also detected and accounted for by the applied filters (see sec. 6.3.1). However, it
was clearly not just a "line", but a wide range of accumulated noise that, even after applying
low-order filters, would affect the CCA values. The default filters used by the OpenBCI GUI
includes a 4-order Butterworth bandpass filter from 5-50 Hz including a notch filter at 50 and 60
Hz of unknown order and bandwidth. Due to the low filter order and a bandpass up to maximum
50 Hz, the broad "power line" at 50 Hz is typically not visible. This greatly the range of EEG
signals that can be examined using OpenBCI.
It was observed during the initial testing of the OpenBCI device that very small vibrations of the
electrode cables led to extreme noise and that connecting the OpenBCI dongle to a hub instead
of directly to the PC would greatly reduce the unexpected packet loss, which were visualized
as bursts (see sec. E.1). As mentioned in sec. 4.3.2 suggestions on how to reduce noise were
followed during data collection to reduce technical related noise such as cable movements, low
battery and the dongle connected directly to the PC. Despite the instructions given to the
participants, it was not always possible for the subject to not move at all and always avoid eye
blinks. However, this was the same case for g.tec. It was investigated whether ICA and the
detection and removal of eye-blinks and heart beats would improve the results, but led to no
difference in the accuracies and instead increased the computation. An automatic ICA was also
designed to remove excessive noise based on a simple statistical outlier principle, but it was not
possible to tell whether important EEG information was removed just because the amplitude
was stronger at one channel than the remaining channels. Therefore, it was not included in this
project. Instead, an investigation was carried out into whether the signal quality of OpenBCI
could be improved. This is described in sec. 8.2.1.
Page 73 of 77
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 8. Discussion
8.2. OpenBCI findings
8.2.1
OpenBCI - search for improvements
A study on how to improve the EEG signals using OpenBCI was made and evaluated on the
visibility of alpha waves. Lower frequencies were tested with the same setup (with and without
conductive gel) in both the office room and the same lab as g.tec, but no visible peaks or alpha
waves were found. A major concern was the location of the reference electrode at AFz. To change
its position and still retain the other electrodes in the cap, a gold electrode was connected to
the Cyton-Daisy board and placed at the right earlobe. This resulted in all the electrodes being
marked as railed on the OpenBCI GUI due to the combination of different electrode types.
The final conclusion was to use OpenBCI gold cup electrodes mounted at FpZ (ground), ear
lobe (reference), Oz and O2 with Ten20 conductive paste, including tape and a head band for
stability. The experiment was done in the g.tec lab (PHY-2 at DTU) using just the Cyton board
to have a sampling frequency of 250 Hz. The illustration of the setup is shown in Figure 8.3a
next to the alpha waves visualized in the OpenBCI GUI in Figure 8.3.
(a)
(b)
Figure 8.3: Two figures (a) and (b). (a) shows the OpenBCI setup using gold electrodes placed
at Oz and O2. The ground and reference electrode are positioned at FpZ and the earlobe, re-
spectively. (b) shows the EEG signals measured during the setup and visualized in the OpenBCI
GUI with a Butterworth bandpass filter from 5-50 Hz, order 4, and two notch filters at 50 and
60 Hz. The sampling frequency was 250 Hz.
The detection of alpha waves proved that it is possible to detect low-amplitude signals at the
same level as SSVEP using the default OpenBCI filters. The setup did not include an EEG cap,
which made it generally more time-consuming and difficult to mount the electrodes, especially
in the exact same positions. Nevertheless, this did not avoid the random packet loss visualized
as bursts in the signals. In offline analysis these can be removed manually, but it is pointless
if the perspective is an online analysis. Otherwise, there are no wires connected to the PC and
the low price would be ideal for home use. Table 8.1 compares the prices of the OpenBCI setup
versus the g.tec setup, where it is seen that g.tec is almost 8 times the price of OpenBCI.

The investigation managed to improve the signal quality.
Nevertheless, the packet loss, the
limited frequency range (practically speaking between 5-50 Hz) and the challenge of consistent
setup really reduce its potential of use in various EEG experiments.
8.3
Future research perspectives
This section presents a number of adjustments and future developments of this project to get
closer to a fully functional BCI for smart home control.
Setup. A hybrid SSVEP-ASSR setup was found to perform worse than the classical SSVEP
experiment. Therefore, a shorter detection time and high accuracy are more likely to be found
in a different setup. Since the parallel setup and the addition of auditory stimuli only increased
the detection complexity, a non-interfering system is of interest. The good results in the SSVEP
experiment makes it suitable for a BCI and could e.g. be combined with eye-gaze as suggested
by other papers [18]. Future experiments should be done in a home-like setup to truly look at
how signal quality and classification can be improved.
Flickers.
The evaluation of the flicker comfort states that this needs to be improved.
An
investigation on finding the perfect combination of shape and higher frequencies would interesting
to achieve great comfort and high accuracy. Adding a checkerboard pattern to the flickers may
further increase accuracy.
Device. The g.tec USBamp achieved good results, but is not suitable for home use in terms of
wires, gel and price. An alternative is the g.tec Unicorn Hybrid Black, which can be used either
dry or wet, has 8 electrodes, sampled with 24 bits and 250 Hz per channel. It is suitable for home
use in terms of price ($1,044 USD) and its light weight to reduce motion artifacts [58]. Finally, it
is wireless and supports the Python package Brainflow, so everything can be controlled directly
from Python. It needs to be investigated whether data loss and the noise level is a problem like
with OpenBCI. For actual patient use, the results from Figure 7.8 (d) and the ability to detect
SSVEP response at the T4 electrode make the UNEEG Medical implant very interesting. The
subcutaneous implant (minimally invasive sqEEG) from UNEEG Medical is affected by less noise
and therefore it is very interesting to investigate its performance on SSVEP-based BCIs.
Features. The CNN model is capable of automatic feature extraction, limiting the need for human
intervention. However, Table 7.11 showed that it was unable to extract relevant features from
the raw time signals, but needs to be converted to PSD values. The default points of overlap
between segments were used (50%), but an investigation should be conducted to see if a different
percent overlap would increase accuracy.
Page 75 of 77
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 8. Discussion
8.3. Future research perspectives
More data. The data sets have been relatively small and limited by the time available. A future
study should include more subjects and more data for training the classification algorithms.
Adaptive classifier/segment length. Some subjects in the UD scenario in Table 7.7 showed ac-
curacies of up to 100%. For them, the segment length could easily be shortened to reduce the
detection time, but only for a non-generic SSVEP-based BCI. An adaptive algorithm is of interest
so that it finds the lowest and acceptable detection time for the individual subject.
BCI GUI. The SSVEP data collection GUI that was created during this project is maturing from
a tool for creating flicker stimuli to collect and classify SSVEP recordings. The next step would
be to make it predict on online recordings and connect it to actual smart home devices, test it
on various subjects and use their feedback to improve the GUI.
Page 76 of 77
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 9
Conclusions
This project investigated whether it was possible to design a setup that does not rely solely
on the visual cortex and also uses higher frequencies to improve comfort and reduce the risk
of triggering epileptic seizures. For this reason, a SSVEP-ASSR-based setup was created for
a three-class problem using flicker frequencies at 12, 20 and 30 Hz and auditory signals with
envelope modulation frequencies of 35, 40 and 45 Hz.
An affordable and mobile acquisition
device was of interest as the perspective was smart home use for users who cannot overcome the
simplest activities of the day. Thus, OpenBCI was initially tested and later compared with g.tec.
The main results are summarized as follows.
First, OpenBCI was found to have too many issues in terms of packet loss and excessive noise.
The accuracies were all what would be considered random. Further investigation showed that it
was possible to improve the signal quality, but required a more time-consuming setup than g.tec
and also did not fully prevent packet loss from happening. Final conclusions between the SSVEP,
ASSR and SSVEP-ASSR experiments could therefore only be drawn from the recordings from
g.tec.
The parallel-acting hybrid SSVEP-ASSR setup was not ideal for shortening the detection time.
Instead, it increased the classification complexity and led to lower accuracies than found for the
same system with only SSVEP. This was also reflected by the feedback, where the difficulty
of focusing on just one sound received an average score of 7.64 ± 2.14, with 10 being very
difficult.
Neither the CNN nor the SVM model was able to return high-performing metrics on the hy-
brid SSVEP-ASSR setup (the overall maximum was 60.07 ± 2.07%). However, the CNN model
returned the highest overall and average accuracies in all the experiments (SSVEP, ASSR and
SSVEP-ASSR), and therefore more optimal for the investigated experiments, including its ad-
vantage in automatic feature extraction. The research on the combination of SSVEP and ASSR
gave a valuable conclusion about not letting two types of stimuli interfere.
Despite the unpromising results on the SSVEP-ASSR setup, the results on the SSVEP setup con-
firmed that SSVEP generalizes well between data sets as seen by the high accuracy on the unseen
subject (maximum accuracy of 86.67±4.71%) and performs even better in a user-dependent setup
(up to 100±0.00% accuracy). The segmentation analysis in sec. 7.3 showed an optimal segment
length of 4.0 s (88.27 ± 3.15% accuracy) using CCA as input, but the UD scenario results high-
light the possibility to shorten the detection time further with an adaptive threshold based on
the actual BCI user.
Finally, a GUI was created to design SSVEP stimulation, do data acquisition and at last classi-
fication. The GUI was very helpful during the verification of SSVEP signals and can hopefully
be used in the future to easily test different stimuli shapes, frequencies and colors to find a
combination that is comfortable for the user and leads to a low detection time.

Informed consent 
Title of Project 
Optimization and classification of EEG data for a simple BCI by using different types of EEG acquisitions. 
 
Investigator: Jonas Lynge Vishart 
 
Technical University of Denmark (DTU) 
 
Department of Biomedical Engineering 
 
+45 31 36 28 12 
 
S174003@dtu.dk 
 
Purpose of the research 
We invite you to participate in this Master’s project research study at DTU, which seeks to develop a more 
appropriate brain-computer interface (BCI) for daily home use. Taking part in this study is entirely 
voluntary. Please read the following information carefully and ask the researcher in case of any question. If 
you decide to participate you must sign this form to show that you want to take part.  
 
The purpose of this study is to investigate if an EEG-based hybrid BCI can be designed to improve a BCI 
system for smart home control. This can in principle be used by anyone but is beneficial for people with 
motor disabilities, as it can give them the ability to perform the simplest activities of the day. The electrical 
impulses will be recorded using non-invasive electrodes placed on the scalp. You will be asked to 
participate over 2 different days, each day has the exact same procedure but uses a different measuring 
device. 
 
Study Procedures 
One day includes 3 experiments using 2 kinds of EEG evoke potential techniques and a combination:  
1. SSVEP-based (steady-state visual evoked potential) 
2. ASSR-based (auditory steady-state response)  
3. SSVEP-ASSR-based 
 
SSVEP: 
This experiment will present you for 3 flickering buttons that flickers with a different frequency on a 
monitor. A total of 20 sessions will be conducted, each with 3 trials (corresponding to the 3 flickers). One 
trial consists of a blank resting period (1.5 s), a target identification period (0.5 s) and a stimulus period (5 
s). A small square is presented on top of one of the three flickers during target identification and will 
identify the next target stimulus. Please shift eye gaze to the target as soon as possible and avoid eye 
blinks during the 5 s stimulation! A rest period of 20 s is included between every session. 
 
ASSR:  
In this experiment you will be asked to keep your eyes open and focus on an arrow in the center of the 
screen, while wearing the electrode cap, a headset and listen to just one sound and direction. A total of 
three directions (left, right, or both left and right) will be used, and each direction has its own specific 
auditory stimulus. The user is asked to only concentrate on target from one side for a total of 10 times. 
Each time all three stimuli will be played (for 3 s), but if the stimulus is not from the direction of interest, it 
should be ignored and considered as non-target. After 10 sessions for one side, a 20 s break is given, and 
the same procedure is repeated for the two other channels. 
 
 
 
A.1
Informed consent
79
==PAGE:94==
 
Page 2 of 2 
SSVEP-ASSR: 
The SSVEP-ASSR data collection will follow the ASSR protocol. Once again, you must listen to one channel 
direction for 10 sessions, but in this case, you should ALSO concentrate your eyes on the flicker located on 
the same side of the screen as the listening side (left, right, middle) for the 10 sessions, even though the 
auditory stimulus changes direction.  
 
During all experiments you will be told to sit on a chair with your hands on the table. The recording will last 
for a total of 30 min (SSVEP: 14 min, ASSR: 8 min, SSVEP-ASSR: 8 min). The whole session is estimated to 
last 45 minutes, including a 3 min break between every experiment, the setup and a briefing and 
debriefing on the study. 
 
Risks and Discomforts 
No foreseeable risks are determined. However, the flickering buttons could evoke photosensitivity-based 
epileptic seizures for people with epilepsy. By signing this document, you confirm that you do not suffer 
from epilepsy, and you participate at your own risk.  
 
Potential Benefits 
There will be no direct benefit to you for your participation in this study. However, the results of this 
research may guide the future development of BCIs for smart home control. 
 
Statement of Confidentiality 
Your research records will be pseudonymization, meaning that your data your data will be labeled with a 
participant number and gender instead of your name. The list that matches your name with the code 
number is only known by the investigator and is not written anywhere. All data and notes from the 
experiment will be stored at the investigator’s laptop, protected by a password. After the hand-in of the 
project, the data will be stored at a USB stick located at the home office of the investigator. The data will 
be deleted upon your request at any time. 
 
Voluntary Participation 
Taking part in this research study is voluntary. If you choose to take part, you have the right to stop at any 
time. If you decide not to participate or withdraw from the study before data collection is completed, your 
data will be returned to you or destroyed.  
 
Signature and Consent 
Your signature below means that you have received this information and have had the opportunity to ask 
questions. You will receive a copy of this consent form. By signing this consent form, you indicate that you 
are voluntary choosing to take part in this research, and that you are free to withdraw at any time, without 
any reason and without cost.

B.1
Genetic algorithm
Just like a neural network, the genetic algorithm is inspired by the brain, the genetic algorithm
is based on an evolutionary approach where it simulates the process of natural selection. It
is used to solve optimization and modeling problems by sequentially selecting, combining, and
varying parameters using mechanisms that resemble biological evolution [54]. First, it starts at
generation 0 with a population of random possible solutions (individuals). Each possible solution
(individual) consists of a combination of parameters in the search space converted into a binary
encoding. The encoding is analogous to the genome of that individual. Afterwards, the natural
selection process begins. A so-called fitness score is assigned to each individual based on their
performance. Individuals with higher fitness scores are more likely to be selected as the parents
of the next generation. Two parents are selected, and their genomes are randomly cut, combined
and mutated which generates new solutions.
83

Appendix C
GUI for EEG data collection
This chapter includes additional images and details of the GUI described in chapter 3. More
details are given in each figure caption.
C.1
Design SSVEP experiment
The GUI includes several warnings to prevent the user from crashing the system by errors. A few
of them are shown in Figure C.1. More details on the variables in the Create SSVEP experiment
window is listed in Table C.1.
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure C.1: Three figures (a), (b) and (c) showing a few possible warnings that may occur if
invalid input is given when the user clicks Create SSVEP experiment in Figure 3.2c.
Input
Description
Grid type
An option between three possible grids seen in Figure 3.3. The grid type sets the
limit for how many flickers can be added to the total stimulus.
Stimulus duration
A float value between 0 and the shortest video duration of the selected flicker
videos.
Sessions
Defines how many sessions the experiment shall consist of. One session is consid-
ered as a set of trials each with some rest time, cue-target identification time and
stimulus presentation (target flicker). A trial is created for every selected flicker,
hence if 3 flickers are selected, there will be three trials in one session.
Cue time
The time for how long the cue identification object (a square) shall be presented
on top of the target flicker.
Cue color
The color of the cue identification object.
Rest trials
The rest time between each trial. A black screen will be shown in the rest time.
Rest sessions
The rest time between each session. An information box will be displayed during
this period informing the user that a new session is about to begin.
Table C.1: A detailed description of the input variables seen in Figure 3.2c.

E.1
Verification of stimuli - OpenBCI
Obvious bursts are visible in Figure E.1a due to packet loss. Those bursts happen randomly and
should therefore be detected by going through all recordings.
(a)
(b)
(c)

Figure E.1: Four figures (a), (b), (c) and (d) all from the Pz electrode. (a) shows the time signal
for the 2nd iteration of the 12 Hz flicker as target while (b) shows the PSD of that signal. Note
that only one flicker was on the screen. (c) shows parts of the signal in (a) where no bursts
occurs. (d) shows the PSD of (c).
It is clear that the bursts create more noise than just the 50 Hz powerline. Figure E.1d shows
that the 50 Hz powerline dominates even without bursts. Therefore a customized ideal filter was
further added (in the same step as the other filters in order to avoid overshooting effect). The
customized ideal filter was created in 3 steps: 1) use a real-valued Fast Fourier transform on the
time signal, 2) set all values between 42-58 Hz to 0, and 3) inverse Fast Fourier transform on the
Fourier data to convert back to time domain. The actual code can be seen below:

E.2
Connection to the Cyton board
In order to connect to the Cyton board from Python, the port serial number must be defined.
In some cases, the pc is able to automatically detect the port, but if not, following code can be
typed in a terminal to get the serial number of the port: system_profiler SPUSBDataType. An
example is seen on Figure E.4.
Figure E.4: An example on how to get the serial number of the port, by typing system_profiler
SPUSBDataType in a terminal.
Page 91
==PAGE:106==
Appendix E. OpenBCI
E.3. Signals after applied filters
E.3
Signals after applied filters
The EEG signals after application of the filters in Figure 6.4 are visualized for subject 4 (not the
same subject as in g.tec) at the 12 Hz presentation in Figure E.5.
(a) Iteration 10
(b) Averaged over iterations
Figure E.5: Two figures (a) and (b) that show the EEG signals of subject 4 (OpenBCI) at 12
Hz flicker stimulation and after application of filters from Fig. 6.4. (a) shows the time signals at
the 10th iteration while (b) shows the PSD signals averaged over iterations visualized with one
standard deviation.
The 50 Hz noise does obviously still appear in Figure E.5. However, a lower order of the IIR
filters cannot be selected, otherwise important amplitudes at 24, 40 and 60 Hz will be extremely
reduced. Two customized ideal filter at f0 = 25 Hz and f0 = 50 Hz were further added for curios-
ity (in the same step as the other filters in order to avoid overshooting effect). The customized
ideal filter was created in 3 steps: 1) use a real-valued Fast Fourier transform on the time signal,
2) set all values between 42-58 Hz to 0, and 3) inverse Fast Fourier transform on the Fourier
data to convert back to time domain. The results are seen in Figure E.6.
Page 92
==PAGE:107==
Appendix E. OpenBCI
E.3. Signals after applied filters
(a) Iteration 10
(b) Averaged over iterations
Figure E.6: Two figures (a) and (b) that show the EEG signals of subject 4 (OpenBCI) at 12 Hz
flicker stimulation and after application of filters from Fig. 6.4 plus additional ideal filters at 25
Hz (width ±0.9 Hz) and 50 Hz (width ±8 Hz). (a) shows the time signals at the 10th iteration
while (b) shows the PSD signals averaged over iterations visualized with one standard deviation.
Even though the EEG signals are a lot better after the 50 Hz noise removal, it is still clear
that frequencies around 25 Hz are dominating, in particular in the P4, T3 and T4 electrode
channel.

262
def
accuracy ( y_true , y_pred , c l a s s _ r e t ) :
263
#For
multi−c l a s s
c l a s s i f i c a t i o n ,
we
use
one
a g a i n s t
a l l
approach
264
cm=confusion_matrix ( y_true , y_pred )
265
FP = cm . sum( a x i s =0) −np . diag (cm)
266
FN = cm . sum( a x i s =1) −np . diag (cm)
267
TP = np . diag (cm)
268
TN = cm . sum ( ) −(FP + FN + TP)
269
Accuracy = (TP+TN) /(TP+TN+FP+FN)
#produces
a
value
f o r
each
c l a s s
270
r e t u r n
Accuracy [ c l a s s _ r e t ] #r e t u r n
only
f o r
one
s e l e c t e d
c l a s s
